<p>and Microsporum produce both micro and macroconidia.</p>
<p>Figure 1.1 shows different types of hyphae and asexual spores.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="classification-of-fungal-infections-mycoses">CLASSIFICATION OF FUNGAL INFECTIONS (Mycoses)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#classification-of-fungal-infections-mycoses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Clinically, human mycotic infections, mycoses, may be grouped into four categories:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-superficial-mycoses">1. Superficial mycoses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-superficial-mycoses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Superficial or cutaneous mycoses are fungal infections that involve the hair, skin or nails without invading any living tissue. These infections are chronic and resistant to treatment, but rarely affect the general health of the patient. <em>Dermatophytes</em>, which include species of <em>Trichophyton</em>, <em>Microsporum</em> and <em>Epidermophyton</em>, are the most common causative agents of superficial mycoses called ringworm. Also included in this group are fungi causing tinea nigra, tinea versicolour and piedra.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="2-subcutaneous-mycoses">2. Subcutaneous mycoses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-subcutaneous-mycoses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Subcutaneous mycoses are infections confined to the subcutaneous tissue without dissemination to distant sites. For example, infections such as mycetoma, chromoblastomycosis or sporotrichosis are subcutaneous mycoses.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="3-systemic-or-deep-seated-mycoses">3. Systemic or deep-seated mycoses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-systemic-or-deep-seated-mycoses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Deep or systemic mycoses may produce systemic involvement and sometimes, can be fatal. They are most commonly acquired by inhalation of spores and may spread from the lung to involve any part of the body. The major systemic mycoses are caused by fungi such as <em>Histoplasma capsulatum</em>, <em>Coccidioides immitis</em>, <em>Paracoccidioides braziliensis</em>, <em>Blastomyces dermatitidis</em>.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="4-opportunistic-mycoses">4. Opportunistic mycoses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-opportunistic-mycoses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The number of fungi included in this group appears to be ever-expanding. These fungi cause infections primarily in immunocompromised patients, usually due to underlying disease or due to immunosuppressive agents. Most of these fungi are saprophytic, living freely in soil or on decaying organic material. Most commonly encountered opportunistic infections include those caused by <em>Aspergillus</em> species, <em>Candida</em> species and, <em>Cryptococcus neoformans</em>.</p>
<p>Table 1.1 briefly shows morphological and clinical classification of medically important fungi.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Chapter 2</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="laboratory-techniques-in-mycology">Laboratory Techniques in Mycology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-techniques-in-mycology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The goals of a clinical mycology laboratory are to isolate and identify correctly the suspected aetiological agents of fungal infections. These goals can be achieved by appropriate collection, prompt transportation and correct processing of specimens.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="collection-and-transportation-of-specimens">COLLECTION AND TRANSPORTATION OF SPECIMENS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#collection-and-transportation-of-specimens" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Sealed, sterile containers should be used for all liquid or moist specimens. As a general rule, the specimens should not be frozen or allowed to dry. They should either be left at room temperature or refrigerated at 4°C. In many instances, specimens not only contain the aetiological agent, but also contain contaminating bacteria. Therefore, antibiotics should be added to decontaminate the specimens. About 20 units of penicillin, 0.04 mg of streptomycin or 0.02 mg of chloramphenicol can be added per millilitre of the specimen.</p>
<p>Table 2.1 shows the clinical specimens and common fungi found of them.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="respiratory-secretions">Respiratory Secretions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#respiratory-secretions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>As most systemic or deep seated fungal infections have a primary focus in the lungs, respiratory secretion is the most common specimen selected for culture. The respiratory secretions include sputum, bronchial washings and tracheal aspirations.</p>
<p>The first early morning sample of sputum is collected after rinsing the mouth vigorously with water. Sputum induction with a heated aerosol saline suspension may be necessary if an adequate specimen cannot be obtained.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="cerebrospinal-fluid-csf-and-other-body-fluids">Cerebrospinal Fluid (CSF) and other Body Fluids<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#cerebrospinal-fluid-csf-and-other-body-fluids" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>CSF is obtained via lumbar puncture when infections of the central nervous system are suspected. At least 3.0 ml of fluid should be collected in a sterile container for adequate mycological studies. Other fluids, such as synovial fluid, should be collected in a sterile container. If it is a bloody specimen, heparin should be used as an anticoagulant.</p>
<p>&gt; <strong>Note</strong>
&gt; CSF should not be refrigerated. It can be left at room temperature or placed in an incubator overnight at 37°C.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="urine">Urine<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#urine" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The first early morning urine specimens are preferred, but random samples are acceptable. Suprapubic aspirates or catheterized specimens can also be collected. Approximately 25–50 ml of urine should be collected aseptically in a sterile, screw capped container and sent immediately for processing. If a delay of more than 2 hours is an</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Clinical specimens</th><th>Principle genera of pathogenic fungi frequently encountered (alphabetical order)</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Blood</td><td>Candida, Cryptococcus, Histoplasma, Torulopsis.</td></tr><tr><td>Bone and bone marrow</td><td>Blastomyces, Cryptococcus, Histoplasma</td></tr><tr><td>Cerebrospinal fluid</td><td>Candida, Coccidioides, Cryptococcus, Histoplasma</td></tr><tr><td>External auditory canal debris</td><td>Aspergillus, Candida</td></tr><tr><td>Hair</td><td>Microsporum, Piedraia, Trichophyton, Trychosporon</td></tr><tr><td>Joint fluids</td><td>Blastomyces, Coccidioides, Sporothrix</td></tr><tr><td>Mucocutaneous tissue</td><td>Candida, Paracoccidioides</td></tr><tr><td>Nails</td><td>Aspergillus, Candida, Epidermophyton, Microsporum, Trichophyton</td></tr><tr><td>Nasal tissue</td><td>Absidia, Aspergillus, Mucor, Rhinosporidium, Rhizopus</td></tr><tr><td>Prostate fluid</td><td>Blastomyces, Coccidioides</td></tr><tr><td>Skin</td><td>Candida, Blastomyces, Chromoblastomycosis (Cladosporium, Fonsecaea, Philalophora), Coccidioides, Cryptococcus (rare), Epidermophyton, Histoplasma, Malassezia (in direct mounts only), Microsporum, Mycetoma (Acremonium), Trichophyton</td></tr><tr><td>Sputum and bronchial washings</td><td>Aspergillus, Blastomyces, Candida, Coccidioides, Cryptococcus, Geotrichium, Histoplasma, Mucor, Paracoccidioides, Rhizopus, Sporothrix</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>ticipated, the urine specimen should be placed at 4°C to inhibit the overgrowth of bacteria.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="prostatic-fluid">Prostatic Fluid<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prostatic-fluid" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Some fungi such as <em>Blastomyces</em> or <em>Coccidioides</em> can be isolated from prostatic fluid. The bladder is first emptied and prostatic fluid is obtained after massage. It is advisable to inoculate it directly on appropriate isolation media.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="exudates">Exudates<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#exudates" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Exudate from an undrained abscess should be aseptically aspirated with a sterile needle and syringe after disinfecting the skin. Pus from miliary abscess is collected by expressing this material after opening the abscess with a sterile scalpel. The exudates should be collected in sterile tubes and sent to the laboratory immediately.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="skin-scrapings-stratum-corneum">Skin Scrapings (Stratum corneum)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#skin-scrapings-stratum-corneum" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><em>Dermatophytes</em> (<em>Trichophyton</em>, <em>Microsporum</em>, <em>Epidermophyton</em> species) typically cause ringworm lesions of the skin with vesicular, erythematous margin. Briskly clean the lesion to include the entire periphery, using 70% ethanol. If the lesion is inflamed or contains fissures, a yeast infection should be suspected and sterile distilled water should be used to clean the lesion instead of 70% ethanol. With a sterile scalpel, scrape material from the periphery of the lesion. If moist or dried exudate is present on the lesion, it must be collected. The lesions caused by <em>Candida</em> species are usually moist, erythematous and painful.</p>
<p>Skin scrapings should be transported to the clinical laboratory in petri dishes, in paper envelopes or between two microscope slides.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="nails">Nails<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nails" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Collect the nail scrapings either by scraping the nails or by snipping off the infected portion of the nail using sterile scissors. Before scraping, clean the nail with 70% ethanol. Scrape the nail from the proximal to the distal end of the nail. Discard the first 4-5 scrapings, and collect the later ones. The debris under the nail plate should also be collected. Place the nail scrapings in a sterile petri dish, between two microscope slides or in a clean envelope.</p>
<p>&gt; <strong>Note</strong>
&gt; Never use swabs to collect dermatological specimens because fibres can be confused with hyphae in the specimens.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="hair">Hair<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hair" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Hair should be collected from areas of scaling or alopecia (loss of hair). Using sterile forceps, collect dull, broken hairs from the margin of the lesion. Infected hairs are typically loose in the follicles.</p>
<p>Selection of infected hair is facilitated by exposure to filtered ultraviolet light using Wood's lamp. Collect the hair that fluoresce when examined with Wood's lamp. If removal of hairs is difficult, as it may be in young patients, place a strip of clear tape over the lesion and then remove it.</p>
<p>Hairs are transported to the laboratory in the same way as skin and nails.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="tissue-specimens">Tissue Specimens<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tissue-specimens" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The tissue specimens must contain both the centre and the edge of the lesion. This is especially important for recovering dimorphic fungi because <em>Histoplasma capsulatum</em> is usually found in the centre of the lesion while <em>Blastomyces dermatitidis</em> is found at the periphery.</p>
<p>The tissue should not be frozen or allowed to dehydrate before culture. For this reason, freshly collected tissue specimens should be either placed between two sterile moist gauze pads or in a sterile container containing 1-2 ml of sterile saline for immediate transportation the laboratory.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1056 Medical Mycology</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="blood">Blood<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#blood" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>In fungal septicaemia 8.0 ml of intravenous blood should be collected aseptically in a sterile tube containing 0.05% Liquoid (1.7 ml of 0.35% sodium polyanethol sulphonate).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="bone-marrow">Bone Marrow<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#bone-marrow" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Approximately 0.2-0.3 ml of bone marrow is collected aseptically in a heparinized syringe from the sternum or iliac crest.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="direct-examination-of-specimens">DIRECT EXAMINATION OF SPECIMENS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#direct-examination-of-specimens" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>When the properly labelled clinical specimen has been received, it should be grossly examined for the presence of particles; caseous, purulent or bloody areas; and necrotic material. These materials are selectively examined microscopically depending on the type of specimen; and then inoculated onto appropriate culture media.</p>
<p>Direct examination of the specimen is valuable in establishing the provisional diagnosis. For example, isolation of a fungus such as <em>Rhizopus</em> is significant when the hyphae compatible with it have been observed in the direct examination of the specimen. <em>Rhizopus</em> can otherwise be considered as a contaminant.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="techniques-for-direct-examination">TECHNIQUES FOR DIRECT EXAMINATION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#techniques-for-direct-examination" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Techniques commonly used for the direct examination of specimens in the laboratory are:</p>
<ol>
<li>Potassium hydroxide (KOH) preparation.</li>
<li>Negative staining using India ink or 10% nigrosin.</li>
<li>Giemsa stain.</li>
<li>Periodic acid-Schiff (PAS) stain.</li>
<li>Other staining methods such as Gram stain, Acid-fast stain, calcofluor white stain, methanamine silver stain and Papanicolaou stain can be used for detection of specific fungi or agents other than fungi.</li>
</ol>
<p>Table 2.2 lists the techniques useful for the direct microscopic detection of fungi in clinical specimens.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="potassium-hydroxide-koh-preparation">POTASSIUM HYDROXIDE (KOH) PREPARATION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#potassium-hydroxide-koh-preparation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The KOH preparation is ideal for determining fungal morphology because even though it readily dissolves the clinical material, fungal elements remain intact for a longer period due to the chitin in their cell walls. The clearing effect can be enhanced by gently warming the preparation over a Bunsen burner or a hot plate. The KOH preparation, besides helping in identification of fungi, facilitates selection of appropriate culture media.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="reagent">Reagent<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#reagent" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Prepare a 10% (w/v) solution of KOH. A few drops of permanent blue-black ink may be added to the solution to highlight fungal walls.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="technique">Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Place a drop of 10% KOH on a clean glass slide. Place the material to be examined in the KOH. Tease the material apart using two dissecting needles. Place a cover slip over it. Additional KOH may be necessary, and can be added at the edge of the cover slip. If excess, it should be removed by blotting with a filter paper.</p>
<p>Allow the preparation to stand at room temperature till it is cleared. Alternatively, pass the slide quickly through a bunsen flame to accelerate clearing and expel air bubbles. Observe microscopically using low intensity of light or phase-contrast microscopy. Figure 2.1 shows dermatophyte hyphae in the KOH mount of skin and nail scraping.</p>
<p><strong>Note</strong></p>
<ol>
<li>The clearing action of KOH will continue until the fungi and the tissue are completely destroyed. It may be preserved for a few days by the addition of a drop of glycerol to the coverslip preparation.</li>
</ol>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Laboratory Techniques in Mycology 1057</p>
<p>Table 2.2 Methods of direct examination of fungal specimens</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Method</th><th>Use</th><th>Advantages</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Acid-fast stain</td><td>Detection of acid fast bacilli such as <em>Mycobacterium</em> and <em>Nocardia</em> species</td><td>Helps in the identification of <em>Nocardia</em>* and <em>B. dermatitidis</em></td></tr><tr><td>Calcofluor white</td><td>Detection of fungi</td><td>Can be mixed with KOH; detects fungi rapidly because of bright fluorescence</td></tr><tr><td>Gram stain</td><td>Detection of bacteria, yeast</td><td>Is commonly performed on most clinical specimens submitted for bacteriology and will detect most fungi, if present.</td></tr><tr><td>India ink</td><td>Detection of <em>C. neoformans</em> in CSF</td><td>When positive in CSF, is diagnostic of cryptococcal meningitis</td></tr><tr><td>Potassium hydroxide (KOH)</td><td>Clearing of specimens to make fungi more readily visible</td><td>Rapid detection of fungal elements</td></tr><tr><td>Methenamine silver stain</td><td>Detection of fungi in histological section</td><td>Best stain to detect fungal elements</td></tr><tr><td>Periodic acid-Schiff (PAS) stain</td><td>Detection of fungi</td><td>Stains fungal elements well; hyphae of moulds and yeasts can be readily distinguished.</td></tr><tr><td>Giemsa stain</td><td>Examination of bone marrow or peripheral blood smears</td><td>Detects <em>H. capsulatum</em></td></tr></tbody></table>
<ul>
<li>Acid-fast bacterium</li>
</ul>
<ol start="2">
<li>For the specimen of nails, add 10g of KOH to 40% solution of dimethyl sulphoxide (DMSO).</li>
</ol>
<p>Table 2.3 lists the use of direct examination for presumptive identification of some commonly encountered fungi.</p>
<p>◆ NEGATIVE STAINING USING INDIA INK OR 10% NIGROSIN</p>
<p>For specimens such as cerebrospinal fluid, the negative staining procedure using India ink or 10% nigrosin is very useful. In this technique, ink is used as a dark background to highlight the hyaline yeast cells and capsular material around them. Figure 2.2 shows capsules of <em>Cryptococcus neoformans</em>.</p>
<p>Reagent</p>
<p>10% nigrosin or India ink.</p>
<p>Technique</p>
<ul>
<li>To a small loopful of saline on a clean slide, add a minute amount of growth from a young agar culture with an inoculating loop and mix. Cerebrospinal fluid may be used directly or as a centrifuged deposit.</li>
<li>Add a small loopful of India ink or 10% nigrosin, mix and immediately cover with a coverslip. Press the coverslip down to express excess ink.</li>
<li>Examine under the oil immersion objective.</li>
<li>Capsules, if present, stand out as clear halos around the yeast cells against a dark background.</li>
</ul>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1058 Medical Mycology</p>
<p>Table 2.3 Characteristic features of fungi in direct examination</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Direct Microscopic Observations</th><th>Presumptive Identification</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Hyphae relatively small (3μm-6μm) and regular in size, dichotomously branching at 45-degree angles with distinct cross-septa.</td><td>Aspergillus species</td></tr><tr><td>Hyphae irregular in size, ranging from 6μm to 50μm, ribbon-like, and devoid of septa</td><td>Zygomycetes (Phycomycetes): Rhizopus, Mucor, Absidia species</td></tr><tr><td>Hyphae small (2μm-3μm) and regular, some branching, with rectangular arthrospores sometimes seen; found only in skin, nail scrapping and hair</td><td>Dermatophyte group, Microsporum species, Trichophyton species, Epidermophyton species</td></tr><tr><td>Delicate branching filaments (1μm-1.5μm in diameter), sometimes contained within "sulphur granules" particularly with Actinomyces species; gram-positive. Species of Nocardia are partially acid-fast.</td><td>Actinomycetes group: Actinomyces species Nocardia species Streptomyces species</td></tr><tr><td>Hyphae, distinct points of constriction simulating linked sausages (pseudohyphae), with budding yeast forms (blastospores) often seen.</td><td>Candida species</td></tr><tr><td>Yeast forms, cell spherical and variable in size (5μm-20μm), classically with a thick polysaccharide capsule (not all cells are encapsulated), with one or more buds attached by a narrow constriction</td><td>Cryptococcus neoformans Cryptococcus species (nonencapsulated)</td></tr><tr><td>Small budding yeast, relatively uniform in size (3μm-5μm), with a single bud attached by a narrow base, extracellular or inside macrophages</td><td>Histoplasma capsulatum</td></tr><tr><td>Yeast forms, large (8μm-20μm), with a thick, double-contoured wall, with a single bud attached by broad base</td><td>Blastomyces dermatitidis</td></tr><tr><td>Large, yeast cells (8μm-20μm) with multiple daughter buds with narrow base around periphery (mariner's wheel)</td><td>Paracoccidioides brasiliensis</td></tr><tr><td>Large, irregularly sized (10μm-50μm), thick walled spherules, many of which contain small (2μm-4μm) round endospores</td><td>Coccidioides immitis</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="giemsa-stain">Giemsa Stain<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#giemsa-stain" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Giemsa stain is excellent for demonstrating the presence of intracellular yeast cells of <em>Histoplasma capsulatum</em> in specimens such as bone marrow or peripheral blood. The intracellular yeast forms of <em>H. capsulatum</em> stain light to dark blue with Giemsa stain.</p>
<p>For preparation and staining technique of Giemsa stain, refer to Haematology Section.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="periodic-acid-schiff-pas-stain">Periodic acid Schiff (PAS) Stain<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#periodic-acid-schiff-pas-stain" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In some specimens such as nail or skin, fungi are not readily visible in the clinical specimens by KOH preparation. PAS staining is a very useful technique for the detection of fungi.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Laboratory Techniques in Mycology 1059</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-a59ccf13.jpeg" alt="img-11.jpeg">
Fig. 2.2 Cryptococcus neofermans: India ink preparation of CSF</p>
<p>(i) For dermatological specimens, spread albumin fixative on the slide before making a smear to fix the tissue.
(ii) Place the smear on a slide warmer for two-three hours before staining, or pass through a flame to warm it.
(iii) The periodic acid and the metabisulphite solutions must be freshly prepared.
(iv) For the details of preparation of reagents and staining technique, refer to Histopathology Section.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="5-other-staining-methods">5. Other Staining Methods<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-other-staining-methods" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Gram stain (refer to Bacteriology section) is useful for demonstrating the presence of Gram positive filaments of Actinomycetes; and yeasts such as Candida species.</p>
<p>Acid-fast stain (refer to Bacteriology section) using 1% sulphuric acid for decolourization, can detect Nocardia species.</p>
<p>Calcofluor white fluorescent stain can detect fungi very easily as they fluoresce under UV light. Methanamine silver stain or Papanicolaou stain (refer to Histopathology section) can also detect fungi, especially in tissues; but need elaborate staining facility and is time consuming.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="8897930a"><span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo>⋄</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\diamond</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.4445em;"></span><span class="mord">⋄</span></span></span></span></span> COMMON CULTURE MEDIA FOR FUNGI<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#8897930a" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>There are approximately 30 different types of media routinely used in clinical laboratories for the recovery of medically important fungi. The selection of media to be used depends on the knowledge of the fungi that are typically recovered from that particular geographical region. Two general types of culture media are essential for the primary recovery of fungi from clinical specimens.</p>
<p>An ideal combination of media for the primary recovery of pathogenic fungi is a non-selective medium, which will permit the growth of virtually all the fungi; and a selective medium, which contains antibiotics such as chloramphenicol and cycloheximide to prevent bacterial and to some extent, fungal contaminants. An enriched medium such as brain heart infusion agar (BHIA) with or without sheep blood is used for the recovery of more fastidious dimorphic fungi such as Histoplasma capsulatum or Blastomyces dermatitidis.</p>
<p>The purpose and composition of some commonly used media in mycology are described as follows.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1060 Medical Mycology</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="1-sabouraud-dextrose-agar-sab">1. Sabouraud Dextrose Agar (SAB)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-sabouraud-dextrose-agar-sab" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The original Sabouraud's agar contains 4% glucose and the pH of the medium is more acidic (pH 5.6) than the modified formula. This medium is ideal for studying colonial morphology of dermatophytes and other moulds. Various antimicrobial agents, such as chloramphenicol, gentamicin, penicillin or streptomycin have been added by various mycologists to SAB to avoid bacterial contamination from clinical specimens. Addition of cycloheximide inhibits growth of yeasts and some moulds (e.g. Zygomycetes); and increases chances of recovery of dimorphic fungi such as <em>Histoplasma capsulatum</em> or <em>dermato-phytes</em> such as <em>Trichophyton</em> species.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="composition">Composition<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#composition" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Peptone</th><th>10.0 g</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Glucose</td><td>40.0 g</td></tr><tr><td>Agar</td><td>15.0 g</td></tr><tr><td>Distilled water</td><td>1 litre</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Mix the ingredients and bring the medium to the boil. Adjust the pH to 5.6. Dispense in 7.0 ml aliquots in medium sized (16 × 125 mm) test tubes and autoclave for 10 min at 15 psi. Set the medium as slants.</p>
<p>&gt; <strong>Note</strong> The medium can also be poured into petri-dishes.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="modification-of-sabouraud-s-agar-with-cycloheximide-and-chloramphenicol">Modification of Sabouraud's agar with cycloheximide and chloramphenicol<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#modification-of-sabouraud-s-agar-with-cycloheximide-and-chloramphenicol" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="composition">Composition<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#composition" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Peptone</th><th>10.0 g</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Glucose</td><td>20.0 g</td></tr><tr><td>Cycloheximide</td><td>0.5 g</td></tr><tr><td>Chloramphenicol</td><td>0.5 g</td></tr><tr><td>Agar</td><td>15.0 g</td></tr><tr><td>Distilled water</td><td>1 litre</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Dissolve cycloheximide in 10.0 ml of acetone. Dissolve chloramphenicol in 10.0 ml of 95% ethanol. Mix all the ingredients including cycloheximide and chloramphenicol solutions. Adjust pH to 6.5 and proceed as for original Sabouraud's agar.</p>
<p>&gt; <strong>Note</strong> This medium is available commercially as Mycobiotic agar and Mycosel agar. The media are used for the selective recovery of dermatophytes and dimorphic fungi. Zygomycetes are unable to grow on it.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="2-brain-heart-infusion-agar-bhia">2. Brain Heart Infusion Agar (BHIA)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-brain-heart-infusion-agar-bhia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The BHIA is an enriched medium commonly used as a complement to SAB agar. It contains cycloheximide. The medium helps in the recovery of opportunistic and dimorphic pathogens. Penicillin and streptomycin may be incorporated in the medium to inhibit bacterial contamination. Many dimorphic fungi develop their yeast forms at 37°C on this medium. The BHIA is not used for skin, hair or nail specimens.</p>
<p>&gt; <strong>Note</strong> This medium may be further enriched by the addition of 5% sheep or horse blood, when it is called Brain heart infusion blood agar (BHIBA).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="composition">Composition<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#composition" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Calf brain infusion</th><th>200.0 g</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Beef heart infusion</td><td>250.0 g</td></tr><tr><td>Peptone</td><td>10.0 g</td></tr><tr><td>Glucose</td><td>2.0 g</td></tr><tr><td>Sodium chloride</td><td>5.0 g</td></tr><tr><td>Disodium phosphate</td><td>2.5 g</td></tr><tr><td>Agar</td><td>15.0 g</td></tr><tr><td>Distilled water</td><td>1 litre</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>pH 7.4</p>
<p>Mix the reagents and bring to the boil. Dispense in 7.0 ml aliquots in test tubes (16 × 125 mm) autoclave for 15 minutes at 15 psi. Allow to set as slants.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="3-brain-heart-infusion-biphasic-blood-culture-medium">3. Brain Heart Infusion Biphasic Blood Culture Medium<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-brain-heart-infusion-biphasic-blood-culture-medium" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This medium is used only for the recovery of fungi from blood. For the biphasic medium, prepare a</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Laboratory Techniques in Mycology 1061</p>
<p>slant in Castaneda's bottle using BHIA. After it is set, layer it with sterile BHI broth to cover half the surface of the slant.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-sabhi-agar">4. Sabhi Agar<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-sabhi-agar" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Sabhi agar, with or without 5% sheep blood, is designed for the recovery of dimorphic fungi such as <em>Histoplasma capsulatum</em> and <em>Blastomyces dermatitidis</em>. The medium is not used for skin, hair and nail specimens. It consists of a combination of BHIA and SAB, supplemented with chloramphenicol.</p>
<p>Composition</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Glucose</th><th>21.0</th><th>g</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Peptone</td><td>5.0</td><td>g</td></tr><tr><td>Proteose peptone</td><td>5.0</td><td>g</td></tr><tr><td>Calf brain infusion</td><td>100.0</td><td>g</td></tr><tr><td>Beef heart infusion</td><td>125.0</td><td>g</td></tr><tr><td>Sodium chloride</td><td>2.5</td><td>g</td></tr><tr><td>Disodium phosphate</td><td>1.25</td><td>g</td></tr><tr><td>Chloramphenicol</td><td>0.100</td><td>g</td></tr><tr><td>Agar</td><td>15.0</td><td>g</td></tr><tr><td>Distilled water</td><td>900.0</td><td>ml</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Whole blood (Sheep or fresh human) 100 ml (Optional)</p>
<p>Mix the reagents except chloramphenicol and blood. Autoclave for 15 minutes at 15 psi and cool to 50-52°C.</p>
<p>Dissolve chloramphenicol in 1-2 ml of sterile distilled water and add to the medium. Add whole blood to the medium under aseptic conditions and dispense in sterile test tubes and set as slants.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="5-blood-agar">5. Blood Agar<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-blood-agar" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This medium is used for the recovery of opportunistic and dimorphic pathogens, and not for those from hair, skin and nails. Antimicrobial agents are usually incorporated to avoid bacterial contamination.</p>
<p>For the composition and preparation of blood agar, refer to Bacteriology section.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="6-malt-extract-agar">6. Malt Extract Agar<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#6-malt-extract-agar" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Weak media, such as malt extract agar, which lack high concentrations of sugars, stimulate the development of conidia or spores; and therefore provide means to obtain useful information for the identification of fungi.</p>
<p>Composition</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Malt extract</th><th>20.0 g</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Agar</td><td>12.0 g</td></tr><tr><td>Distilled water</td><td>400 ml</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Mix agar and distilled water and bring to a boil. Cool, add malt extract and mix. Dispense in test tubes and autoclave at 15 psi for 15 minutes. Allow to set as slants.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="7-yeast-extract-phosphate-agar">7. Yeast Extract Phosphate Agar<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#7-yeast-extract-phosphate-agar" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This medium is mainly used for the isolation of dimorphic fungi.</p>
<p>Composition</p>
<p>Solution A</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Yeast extract</th><th>1.0 g</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Agar</td><td>15.0 g</td></tr><tr><td>Distilled water</td><td>1 L</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Solution B</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Disodium hydrogen phosphate, anhydrous</th><th>4.0 g</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Potassium dihydrogen phosphate</td><td>6.0 g</td></tr><tr><td>Distilled water</td><td>30 ml</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Adjust pH to 6.0 with either 1N HCl or 1N NaOH. Boil solution A to dissolve the ingredients. Add 2.0 ml of solution B. Autoclave at 15 psi for 15 minutes and pour into plates or set as slants.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="processing-of-clinical-specimens">PROCESSING OF CLINICAL SPECIMENS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#processing-of-clinical-specimens" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-microscopic-examination">1. Microscopic Examination<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-microscopic-examination" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>(i) Routine: As a routine, the specimen of suspected fungal infection should be examined by the 10% KOH mount microscopically. The presence of fungal</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1062 Medical Mycology</p>
<p>elements helps in the presumptive identification of the causative fungi (Table 2.3).</p>
<p>(ii) Fluid specimens Fluid specimens should be centrifuged and the deposit should be examined in 10% KOH.</p>
<p>(iii) Viscous specimens For viscous specimens such as sputum or prostatic fluid, an equal volume of freshly prepared digestant N-acetyl-L-lysine (NALC or sputalysin) should be used to homogenise the sample before centrifugation.</p>
<p>(iv) Nails If nails do not dissolves in 10% KOH on passing over a flame, they may be left in the KOH overnight in a petri dish with a piece of moist paper (moist chamber) to prevent dehydration.</p>
<p>(v) Tissues with granules Granule containing tissue specimens should be teased in a petri dish to separate the granules. Note the colour, size and consistency of the granules which help in the presumptive identification of the causative agent.</p>
<p>Wash the granules in sterile distilled water. Crush a few granules between two slides and examine them microscopically. If they show branching, septate hyphae 3 to 7 µm in diameter, it indicates a fungal infection.</p>
<p>If the hyphae are less than 1µm in diameter, the causative agent is likely to be Actinomycetes. Perform modified Ziehl-Neelsen stain on the granules using 1% sulphuric acid. This differentiates between Nocardia (acid-fast) and Actinomyces (non-acid fast).</p>
<p>Suspected bacterial granules should be Gram stained.</p>
<p>(vi) Other stains If the KOH preparation is not satisfactory, other method of direct examination should be employed, for example, PAS, methanamine silver stains.</p>
<p>(vii) Negative stain If the yeast cells are observed in a CSF specimen, a wet preparation with India Ink or 10% nigrosin should be made to detect Cryptococcus with large capsules.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="ii-inoculation-onto-media">II. Inoculation Onto Media<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ii-inoculation-onto-media" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>(i) As a general rule, a specimen for superficial fungal culture should be inoculated on two media.</p>
<p>(a) Sabouraud's agar (SAB)</p>
<p>(b) Sabouraud's agar with cycloheximide and chloramphenicol (SAB+C+)</p>
<p>(ii) If subcutaneous or deep seated mycoses are suspected, an enriched medium such as BHIA, BHIBA or Sabhi agar should be used in addition to a and b above.</p>
<p>Table 2.4 shows a general outline for collection, selection of media and processing for various specimens.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="iii-incubation">III. Incubation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#iii-incubation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>(i) The media inoculated with specimen for fungal cultures are incubated at 25-30°C. The cultures are examined every 2-3 days for evidence of growth.</p>
<p>If no growth is observed in four weeks, the specimen is reported as negative for fungi.</p>
<p>(ii) When an infection with a dimorphic fungus is suspected, an enriched medium such as BHIBA should be incubated at 37°C and examined every 2-3 days for at least up to six weeks. If there is growth, examine microscopically for a yeast or tissue form.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="iv-identification-of-isolates">IV. Identification of Isolates<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#iv-identification-of-isolates" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The methods of identifications of the isolated fungus are described in Chapter 3.</p>
<p><strong>Safety precautions</strong> Medically important fungi can be extremely dangerous. It is necessary to select the containers for fungal cultures very carefully. Petri dishes are not recommended (except for yeast extract phosphatase medium) because the media they contain get rapidly dehydrated. They also</p>
