<p>1066 Medical Mycology</p>
<p>create dangerous aerosols when opened. The petri dishes can get easily contaminated. The petri dishes, however, are useful due to their large surface area which makes it easy to reach the fungal growth and observe colonial morphology closely. If used, the petri dishes must be sealed with parafilm or placed in plastic bags to avoid spores being released into the atmosphere and to avoid dehydration. The <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>25</mn><mo>×</mo><mn>125</mn><mrow><mtext>&nbsp;</mtext><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">25 \times 125 \mathrm{~mm}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7278em; vertical-align: -0.0833em;"></span><span class="mord">25</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span><span class="mbin">×</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">125</span><span class="mord"><span class="mspace nobreak">&nbsp;</span><span class="mord mathrm">mm</span></span></span></span></span></span> test tube with screw cap is most ideal because it offers adequate surface area. The screw cap should never be tightly closed to allow gaseous exchange for the growth and sporulation of fungi.</p>
<p><strong>Subculturing of isolates</strong> Subculturing is a propagation technique, ensuring that important isolates are maintained in the laboratory. Isolates may be subcultured in several ways. The simplest procedures is to transfer a small portion of growth from the edge of the old colony including a thin layer of the medium using a sterile scalpel, onto a fresh medium in a petri dish or a test tube. It is recommended to select the inoculum from the edge of the colony where the fungus is vegetatively most active.</p>
<p>When dangerous fungi such as <em>Coccidioides immitis</em> or <em>Aspergillus</em> species which produce abundant conidia are being subcultured, the colony should be flooded with a few millilitres of sterile distilled water to prevent formation of aerosols. This minimises the danger of infection and contamination of other cultures. A suspension formed with water can then be used to inoculate fresh media. All manipulations must be carried out in the safety cabinet.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Chapter 3</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="identification-of-fungal-isolates">Identification of Fungal Isolates<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#identification-of-fungal-isolates" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="i-identification-of-filamentous-fungi-moulds">I. IDENTIFICATION OF FILAMENTOUS FUNGI (MOULDS)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-identification-of-filamentous-fungi-moulds" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Filamentous fungi isolated on laboratory media from the clinical specimens are identified on the basis of:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-the-growth-rate">1. The Growth Rate<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-the-growth-rate" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The time required for a fungus to produce visible growth on a culture medium can be described as its growth rate. Observation of the growth rate of a culture is an important information for its identification, especially when used in combination with other features.</p>
<p>For example, colonies of <em>Zygomycetes</em> such as <em>Mucor</em> or <em>Rhizopus</em> appear within 24 hours and those of other hyaline and dematiaceous saprobes appear in 1-5 days. On the other hand, the growth rate of dimorphic fungi is generally slow, and requires 1-4 weeks for the appearance of visible colonies. Growth rate of some fungi is variable, and may depend upon the size of the inoculum, type of media and temperature of incubation.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="2-colonial-morphology">2. Colonial Morphology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-colonial-morphology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Visual examination of a fungal colony gives important data regarding the physical appearance such as:</p>
<p>(i) Colour The colour may be white, grey, cream, buff, pale orange, brown, deep purple or green.</p>
<p>(ii) Texture The texture may be powdery, cottony, velvety, silky or waxy.</p>
<p>(iii) Colonial topography The fungi can form flat, heaped, grooved, folded or wrinkled colonies.</p>
<p>(iv) Difussible pigments The pigments produced by fungi can be seen on the reverse side of the dish as buff, pale orange, deep yellow, grey, brown or deep red colours.</p>
<p>The observation of these features, supplemented with the growth rate and microscopic appearance, help in the identification of fungi. Although it may be possible to recognise certain species recovered repeatedly in the laboratory based on their colonial morphology, it is not a very reliable criterion for identification. The colonial morphology varies greatly with the type of media used and the temperature of incubation, especially in the case of dimorphic fungi. For example, <em>Histoplasma capsulatum</em> appears as a white to tan mould on brain heart infusion agar (BHIA), but may appear yeast-like when grown on the same medium enriched with blood.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="3-microscopic-morphology">3. Microscopic Morphology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-microscopic-morphology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The microscopic morphological features of most fungi are stable and exhibit minimal variation. The</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Medical Mycology</p>
<p>definitive identification is based on the morphology of the spores and hyphae.</p>
<p>(i) Spores The morphology of spores depends on:
(a) Type of spores, e.g. sporangiospores, chlamydospores, microconidia, macroconidia, etc. (Fig. 1.1).
(b) Characteristic shape, e.g. spindle-shaped macroconidia of Microsporum species
(c) Arrangement of spores on the hyphae The spores may appear singly, in chains, clusters, terminal, flower-like, etc.
(ii) Hyphae The important features of hyphae which help in the identification are:
(a) Size or diameter of hyphae Diameter of 1-2 µm may suggest dimorphic fungi or actinomycetes. Dermatophytes have hyphae ranging from 3-15 µm Rhizopus or Mucor hyphae may be large, ribbon-like, about 25 µm in diameter.
(b) Shape of hyphae The hyphae may show characteristic shapes such as spiral, pectinate, or favic chandeliers (Fig. 5.2).</p>
<p>Dimorphic fungi Dimorphic fungi have the ability to grow at 25°C on routine mycological culture media as moulds. When these fungi are in tissue or on special media incubated at 37°C, they grow either as yeasts or as spherules containing endospores. This ability to grow in two different vegetative forms helps in distinguishing dimorphic fungi from other species. Special techniques, such as exoantigen tests, are also available for confirmation of the identity of a dimorphic fungus.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="additional-identification-tests">Additional Identification Tests<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#additional-identification-tests" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>(i) Hair penetration test Some fungal isolates have the ability to penetrate hair in vitro. This test is useful in distinguishing atypical isolates of dermatophytes, especially Trichophyton rubrum from Trichophyton mentagrophytes.
(ii) Nutritional studies These tests are used to determine the ability of a fungus to utilize a specific nutrient.</p>
<p>(iii) Absence of proteolytic activity This test determines the pathogenicity of certain fungi. It is done by growing the isolates on media containing gelatine or serum.
(iv) Rice grain test Inability of some dermatophytes such as Microsporum audouinii to grow on rice grains is used to differentiate them from other species of the genus, especially Microsporum canis.
(v) Temperature studies Ability of some moulds to grow at higher temperatures is used to differentiate them from other closely related species.
(vi) Urea hydrolysis This test is used to differentiate between Trichophyton mentagrophytes which is urease positive and T. rubrum which is urease negative.</p>
<p>&gt; <strong>Note:</strong> These tests are used when the growth rate, colonial morphology and microscopic morphology does not lead to a confirmed identification of a fungus.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="identification-procedures">IDENTIFICATION PROCEDURES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#identification-procedures" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="i-microscopic-examination">I. Microscopic Examination<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-microscopic-examination" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>Wet mount or tease mount This is the most common technique for microscopic examination of fungi.</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>Reagents</strong> (i) Lactophenol cotton blue</p>
<ul>
<li>Phenol, concentrated 20.0 ml</li>
<li>Lactic acid 20.0 ml</li>
<li>Glycerol 40.0 ml</li>
<li>Cotton blue 0.05 g</li>
<li>Distilled water 20.0 ml.</li>
</ul>
<p>Dissolve cotton blue in distilled water and add phenol, lactic acid and glycerol.
(ii) 95% ethanol</p>
<p><strong>Technique</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>Place a drop of 95% ethanol on a microscope slide.</li>
<li>Using a sterile inoculating needle or fine forceps, gently remove a small portion of</li>
</ul>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Identification of Fungal Isolates 1069</p>
<p>fungal growth from midway between the colony centre and the edge. Take care that both the aerial and substrate (vegetative) mycelium is included in the specimen.</p>
<ul>
<li>With two dissecting needles, tease the fungus gently so that it is thinly spread out in the mounting medium.</li>
<li>When most of the ethanol has evaporated, add a drop of lactophenol cotton blue and cover with a cover glass.</li>
<li>Examine the fungus microscopically.</li>
</ul>
<p>(ii) In a tease mount, conidia or spores are detached from their position on the fungus. Therefore, identification is more difficult with a tease mount than with a slide culture technique or tape technique.</p>
<p>(ii) Ethanol acts as a wetting agent which prevents the formation of air bubbles around the structure to be studied.</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Tape technique This procedure permits the observation of conidia or spores still attached, as well as other structures in their natural position.</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Place one drop of lactophenol cotton blue on a clean slide.</li>
<li>Attach one end of small piece of transparent tape to a round wooden applicator stick and then lightly touch the colony with the sticky surface of the tape.</li>
<li>Loosen the tape and lay it with the surface containing the fungus face down into the mounting medium on the slide.</li>
<li>Detach the tape from the wooden applicator stick.</li>
<li>Examine the mount directly with a microscope.</li>
</ul>
<ol start="3">
<li>Slide culture technique Slide culture technique is used for the identification of moulds which are difficult to identify with tease mounts. Media such as cornmeal agar, potato dextrose agar and cereal agar are preferred for this technique because they enhance spore development. However, Sabouraud's agar can be used if these media are not available.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="step-i-slide-culture-set-up">Step I: Slide culture set up<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#step-i-slide-culture-set-up" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Place one piece of filter paper in a petri dish so that it just covers the bottom.</li>
<li>Place a glass rod that has been bent into a 'V' or 'A' shape on top of the filter paper.</li>
<li>Place a clean glass slide and a cover glass on the bent rod.</li>
<li>Autoclave this set up at 15 psi for 15 minutes.</li>
</ul>
<p>&gt; <strong>Note</strong>
&gt; The same set up can be prepared by assembling the sterile components (filter paper, glass rod, slide, coverslip and petri dish) aseptically.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="step-ii-preparation-of-the-slide-culture">Step II: Preparation of the slide culture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#step-ii-preparation-of-the-slide-culture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>From a plate of the appropriate medium, cut the agar with a sterile scalpel into squares which are 1 cm on each side.</li>
<li>Aseptically transfer one agar block using a sterile scalpel to the microscope slide in the slide culture set-up.</li>
<li>With an inoculating wire, transfer a small amount of the fungus from the edge of the colony to each of the four sides of the agar block.</li>
<li>Aseptically place the coverglass on top of the agar block.</li>
<li>Carefully add 1-2 ml of sterile distilled water to the bottom of the slide culture onto the filter paper. This is necessary to maintain a humid atmosphere in the petri dish, in order to prevent the agar block from drying.</li>
<li>Incubate for about two weeks in the dark at room temperature.</li>
</ul>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1070 Medical Mycology</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="step-iii-examination-of-the-slide-culture">Step III: Examination of the slide culture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#step-iii-examination-of-the-slide-culture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Remove the slide from the set-up.</li>
<li>Place a small drop of lactophenol cotton blue on another slide.</li>
<li>With a pair of forceps, carefully remove the cover glass from the agar block and quickly pass it through a flame once or twice to heat-fix the fungus and the conidia in place.</li>
<li>Place the coverslip on top of the lactophenol cotton blue, the fungus facing downwards.</li>
<li>Examine microscopically.</li>
<li>If there are too many air bubbles, warm the preparation gently over a flame.</li>
</ul>
<p>&gt; <strong>Note</strong>
&gt; It is recommended that the slide culture should be prepared in duplicate so that in case of insufficient sporulation in the first mount, the second one can be examined after further incubation.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="special-techniques">SPECIAL TECHNIQUES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#special-techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-in-vitro-hair-penetration-test">1. In Vitro Hair Penetration Test<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-in-vitro-hair-penetration-test" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The ability of some dermatophytes to infect hair is used as an identification test. The hair infection can be external (ectothrix) or internal (endothrix). When hyphae are seen without spores the infection is known as favus (Fig. 3.1).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="technique">Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Place several fragments of sterile hairs in a sterile petri dish. Blond hair shows the results more clearly.</li>
<li>Add approximately 25 ml of sterile distilled water and 1 ml of 1% sterile yeast extract, to the petri dish.</li>
<li>With a sterile needle, transfer a small amount of fungal growth to the water in the petri dish.</li>
<li>Inoculate known isolates of <em>Trichophyton mentagrophytes</em> and <em>T. rubrum</em> as positive and negative control respectively at the same time in the same way in separate petri dishes.</li>
<li>Incubate at 25°C and examine weekly for up to four weeks by placing a few fragments of hairs on a microscope slide in a drop of lactophenol cotton blue.</li>
<li><em>T. mentagrophytes</em> usually gives a positive result within two weeks. However, the test should not be read as negative until four weeks.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-c144d1cd.jpeg" alt="img-12.jpeg">
(a) Ectothrix</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-4ff9148a.jpeg" alt="img-13.jpeg">
(b) Endothrix</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-f2a260ed.jpeg" alt="img-14.jpeg">
(c) Favus</p>
<p>Fig. 3.1 Ectothrix, endothrix and favus hair infection</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Identification of Fungal Isolates 1071</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-in-vitro-conversion-of-dimorphic-moulds">2. In Vitro Conversion of Dimorphic Moulds<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-in-vitro-conversion-of-dimorphic-moulds" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Dimorphic moulds, except <em>Coccidioides immitis</em>, grow as yeast in tissue and on special media at 37°C. Total conversion from mould to yeast form usually requires rapid, continuous subculturing. However, appearance of a few yeast forms in the first subculture is sufficient for identification.</p>
<p><em>Coccidioides immitis</em> produces spherules and endospores at 37°C when grown on special media in 5-10% carbon dioxide atmosphere.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="a-technique-for-mould-to-yeast-or-spherule-conversion">A. Technique for mould to yeast or spherule conversion<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-technique-for-mould-to-yeast-or-spherule-conversion" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="media-used">Media used<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#media-used" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>(i) Brain heart infusion agar (BHIA) with 5-10% sheep blood.
(ii) Blood agar
(iii) Converse Medium (Levine modification)</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Ammonium acetate</th><th>1.23 g</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Glucose</td><td>4.0 g</td></tr><tr><td>Dipotassium phosphate</td><td>0.52 g</td></tr><tr><td>Potassium phosphate</td><td>0.4 g</td></tr><tr><td>Magnesium sulphate</td><td>0.4 g</td></tr><tr><td>Zinc sulphate</td><td>0.002 g</td></tr><tr><td>Sodium chloride</td><td>0.014 g</td></tr><tr><td>Sodium carbonate</td><td>0.012 g</td></tr><tr><td>Tamol</td><td>0.5 g</td></tr><tr><td>Calcium chloride</td><td>0.002 g</td></tr><tr><td>Agar (Agarose or Purified agar)</td><td>10.0 g</td></tr><tr><td>Distilled water</td><td>1 litre</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Autoclave at 15 psi for 15 minutes and pour in petri dishes.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="technique">Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Perform all the procedures in a biological safety cabinet.</li>
<li>With a long handled inoculating needle, remove a small portion of the isolate to be examined and transfer it to two tubes containing the appropriate medium (Table 2.4) for the suspected dimorphic fungus.</li>
<li>Incubate one tube at 25°C and the other tube at 37°C. If <em>Coccidioides immitis</em> is suspected, incubate at 40°C.</li>
<li>If <em>Sporothrix schenckii</em> or <em>Coccidioides immitis</em> is suspected, incubate the second tube in 5-10% CO₂, either using a candle jar or Gas Pak for CO₂ (refer to Bacteriology).</li>
<li>Incubate for 3-4 weeks, examining after every 2-3 days. Examine the yeast-like creamy colonies microscopically for the presence of typical yeast cells.</li>
<li>If the results are questionable, rapidly subculture the fungus onto a fresh medium. Several weeks may be necessary for complete conversion. The conversion is said to be positive, when typical yeast cells are present, regardless of their number.</li>
<li>For <em>C. immitis</em>, after 5 days incubation, add 10 ml of formalin per litre volume of jar (e.g. 25 ml for a 2.5 litre jar), and then incubate for additional 24 hours so that the fungus is killed before taking the petri dish out. Examine the preparation microscopically for the presence of spherules and released endospores (Fig. 5.7).</li>
<li>Autoclave the culture and other materials used before being discarded.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="b-technique-for-yeast-mould-conversion">B. Technique for yeast mould conversion<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#b-technique-for-yeast-mould-conversion" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Dimorphic fungi in yeast form will form typical mould colonies when the yeast or spherule form is subcultured on Sabouraud’s agar and incubated at 25°C.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-exoantigen-test-for-identification-of-dimorphic-moulds">3. Exoantigen Test for Identification of Dimorphic Moulds<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-exoantigen-test-for-identification-of-dimorphic-moulds" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Exoantigen test is useful in identification of dimorphic fungi when their differentiation on the basis of colonial and microscopic morphology is difficult.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1072 Medical Mycology</p>
<p>The test can also be used for some other fungi such as <em>Aspergillus</em> species.</p>
<p>These fungi produce water soluble specific antigens which can be reacted with their homologous antibodies in a double diffusion agar test system.</p>
<p>A commercial test kit is available for the exoantigen test. Detailed instructions for the test are available with the kit.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="ii-identification-of-yeasts">II. IDENTIFICATION OF YEASTS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ii-identification-of-yeasts" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Yeasts are unicellular fungi which occur as spherical or ellipsoidal cells. On culture, they form smooth, creamy colonies resembling those of bacteria. Based on their ability or inability to reproduce by sexual means, yeasts have been traditionally divided into two groups. The true yeasts are those fungi that reproduce sexually, developing ascospores or basidiospores, when the appropriate conditions are met. In contrast to the true yeast, yeast-like fungi or the imperfect yeasts, reproduce only by asexual means, that is, by budding. Yeast-like fungi grow partly as yeasts, and partly as elongated cells resembling hyphae, called psudohyphae. An example of true yeast is <em>Cryptococcus neoformans</em> and that of yeast-like fungus is <em>Candida albicans</em>.</p>
<p>Identification of yeast is based on the following criteria:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-cultural-characteristics">1. Cultural Characteristics<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-cultural-characteristics" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Yeasts pathogenic to man appear as pasty, opaque and usually white, off-white or beige coloured colonies resembling bacterial colonies. They have a sweet smell resembling that of a ripe apple. Depending upon the medium used, it is occasionally possible to distinguish between species based on their colonial morphology. However, apart from differentiating them from filamentous moulds, colonial morphology is not very useful in identification.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="2-microscopic-morphology">2. Microscopic Morphology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-microscopic-morphology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Microscopic morphology of yeast cells can be studied in various ways depending on the type of microscopic feature to be studied.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="i-temporary-direct-mount-wet-preparation">(i) Temporary direct mount (wet preparation)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-temporary-direct-mount-wet-preparation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<p>Direct mounts are used to study the size and shape of yeast cells, and to observe the presence and absence of other structures such as psuedohyphae or chlamydospores. Temporary direct mount is prepared in distilled water because yeasts tend to settle much faster in water than in mounting media such as lactophenol cotton blue (LPCB).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="technique">Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<ul>
<li>Mix a small portion of the yeast colony with a small drop of distilled water on a clean slide.</li>
<li>Place a clean cover glass over it and observe immediately under the microscope.</li>
<li>Note morphological characteristics of the yeast cells.</li>
</ul>
<p>&gt; <strong>Note</strong>
&gt; A temporary direct mount may also be prepared by using LPCB. Allow the cells to settle for five minutes before examining them microscopically.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="ii-permanent-direct-mount">(ii) Permanent direct mount<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ii-permanent-direct-mount" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<ul>
<li>Prepare a smear from the fungal culture.</li>
<li>Heat fix the smear by gently passing through a bunsen flame 2-3 times.</li>
<li>Place a small drop of LPCB on the fixed smear and cover with a cover glass.</li>
<li>Seal the edges of the cover glass either with DPX or with finger nail polish.</li>
<li>Examine microscopically.</li>
<li>Figure 3.2 shows budding yeast cells.</li>
</ul>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Identification of Fungal Isolates 1073</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-244406a3.jpeg" alt="img-15.jpeg">
Fig. 3.2 Budding yeast cells</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-787c99a4.jpeg" alt="img-16.jpeg">
Fig. 3.3 Candida albicans showing germs tubes</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="iii-germ-tube-test">(iii) Germ tube test<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#iii-germ-tube-test" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>A 'germ tube' is a filamentous extension from a yeast cell, about half the width and three to four times the length of the cell (Fig. 3.3). About 90% of yeasts isolated from clinical material are <em>Candida albicans</em>; and the germ tube test provides a simple and reliable procedure for its tentative identification.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="technique">Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Suspend a small portion of an 18-72 hours old isolated colony of the yeast in 0.5 ml of rabbit, horse or human serum in a test tube.</li>
<li>Repeat the same procedure with a known culture of <em>Candida albicans</em> (Positive control) and <em>Candida tropicalis</em> (Negative control).</li>
<li>Incubate all the tubes at 37°C for 2-3 hours.</li>
<li>Place a drop of the yeast suspension on a glass slide and cover with a cover glass. Read the controls first and then the 'test' for the presence or absence of germ tubes. Only <em>C. albicans</em> produces germtubes within three hours.</li>
</ul>
<p>&gt; <strong>Note</strong>
&gt; If no germ tubes are observed within three hours, other methods such as Dalamau plate technique and biochemical tests should be performed for further identification.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="iv-dalamau-plate-technique">(iv) Dalamau plate technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#iv-dalamau-plate-technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The microscopic morphology of various yeasts on cornmeal agar containing 1% Tween 80 can help to a great extent in their identification. Different genera and species of yeasts produce hyphae, pseudohyphae, chlamydospores, arthroconidia and blastoconidia, while some retain the original yeast form, when grown on this medium at room temperature for 1-2 days. This method of identification is known as Dalamau plate technique.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="medium-for-dalamau-plate">Medium for Dalamau Plate<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#medium-for-dalamau-plate" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="cornmeal-agar-with-tween-80">Cornmeal agar with Tween 80<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#cornmeal-agar-with-tween-80" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Cornmeal</th><th>50.0 g</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Agar</td><td>15.0 g</td></tr><tr><td>Distilled water</td><td>1 litre</td></tr><tr><td>Tween 80</td><td>10.0 ml</td></tr></tbody></table>
<ul>
<li>Mix the cornmeal in 500 ml distilled water.</li>
<li>Heat for one hour or autoclave at 15 psi for 10 minutes.</li>
<li>Filter the suspension through cheese cloth.</li>
<li>Bring the volume up to 1 litre, add agar and Tween 80.</li>
<li>Autoclave at 15 psi for 15 minutes.</li>
<li>Pour in sterile petri dishes.</li>
</ul>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1074 Medical Mycology</p>
<p><strong>Note</strong> This medium can also be used without Tween 80 to enhance sporulation of filamentous fungi.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="technique">Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>With a sterile inoculating wire, pick up a small portion of the yeast colony.</li>
<li>Make three parallel cuts, 1/2 inch apart, into the agar plate, holding the wire at an angle of about 45°C.</li>
<li>Place a sterile coverslip on the surface of the agar to cover the portion of the inoculation streaks.</li>
<li>Incubate the plates at 22–26°C for 24–48 hours.</li>
<li>Examine microscopically after removing the lid, through the cover glass, so that the microscope objective does not get contaminated with the yeast.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Interpretation of dalamau plates</strong> Table 3.1 shows a scheme for the preliminary identification of some common yeasts on Dalamau plate.</p>
<p>Begin the examination by scanning the area under the cover glass. If no filamentous structures are observed on the surface, carefully focus on the submerged growth that tracks along the incisions in the agar. Further examination of the hyphae should be made to distinguish between the true hyphae and pseudohyphae. A true hypha or true mycelium has cells of constant width with square ends whereas a pseudomycelium is composed of cells of varying width with rounded ends (Fig. 3.4).</p>
<p>When a true mycelium is observed, individual hyphae should be checked for the formation of arthroconidia and blastoconidia. If present, it identifies the fungus as <em>Trichosporon</em> species.</p>
<p><strong>Table 3.1 Characteristic features of some yeasts on Dalamau plate</strong></p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Organism</th><th>Arthroconidia</th><th>Blastoconidia</th><th>Pseudohyphae or hyphae</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td><em>Candida albicans</em></td><td>None</td><td>Spherical clusters at regular intervals on pseudohyphae</td><td>Chlamydospores present on pseudohyphae</td></tr><tr><td><em>C. glabrata</em></td><td>None</td><td>Small, spherical and tightly compacted</td><td>None</td></tr><tr><td><em>C. krusei</em></td><td>None</td><td>Elongated; clusters occur at septa of pseudohyphae</td><td>Branched pseudohyphae</td></tr><tr><td><em>C. kefy</em> (pseudotropicalis)</td><td>None</td><td>Elongated, lie parallel to pseudohyphae</td><td>Pseudophyphae present but not characteristic</td></tr><tr><td><em>C. tropicalis</em></td><td>None</td><td>Produced randomly along hyphae or pseudohyphae</td><td>Pseudohyphae present but not characteristic</td></tr><tr><td><em>Cryptococcus</em> species</td><td>None</td><td>Round to oval, vary in size, separated by a capsule</td><td>Rare</td></tr><tr><td><em>Trichosporon</em> species</td><td>Numerous</td><td>May be present but difficult to find</td><td>Septate hyphae present</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Identification of Fungal Isolates 1075</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-523a79d7.jpeg" alt="img-17.jpeg">
Fig. 3.4 (a) True mycelium (hyphae) and (b) Pseudohyphae</p>
<p>Geotrichum species produces few or no blastoconidia.</p>
<p>The production of pseudohyphae, chlamydospores or blastoconidia indicates Candida albicans (Fig. 3.5).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-6a49db07.jpeg" alt="img-18.jpeg">
Fig. 3.5 Candida albicans on Dalamau palte showing pseudohyphae, chlamydospores and blastoconidia (blastospores)</p>
<p>The presence of pseudomycelium and absence of arthrospores indicates other Candida species. Some of these such as C. tropicalis, C. parapsillosis and C. pseudotropicalis can be identified on the basis of the shape and arrangement of the blastoconidia.</p>
<p>Three genera, Cryptococcus, Torulopsis and Rhodotorula fail to produce hyphae and retain their true yeast form. Rhodotorula can be easily identified by the pink colour of its carotenoid pigment. The urease test differentiates Cryptococcus (urease positive) from Torulopsis (urease negative).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="3-biochemical-tests">3. Biochemical Tests<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-biochemical-tests" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>There are various biochemical tests used for the identification of yeasts up to the species level. (Depending on their morphological and cultural characteristics, only those tests which are critical for identification and confirmatory in nature should be performed). The commonly used tests are described below.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="i-assimilation-tests">(i) Assimilation tests<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-assimilation-tests" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Assimilation is the utilization of a carbon source in the presence of oxygen. For example, assimilation (aerobic oxidation) of a sugar such as glucose results in the formation of carbon dioxide and water.</p>
<div class="math math-display"><span class="katex-display"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" display="block"><semantics><mrow><mrow><msub><mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi><mn>6</mn></msub><msub><mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi><mn>12</mn></msub><msub><mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi><mn>6</mn></msub></mrow><mo>+</mo><mn>6</mn><msub><mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi><mn>2</mn></msub><mo>→</mo><mn>6</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi><msub><mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi><mn>2</mn></msub></mrow><mo>+</mo><mn>6</mn><mrow><msub><mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi><mn>2</mn></msub><mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathrm{C_6H_{12}O_6} + 6\mathrm{O_2} \rightarrow 6\mathrm{CO_2} + 6\mathrm{H_2O}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8333em; margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mathrm mtight">12</span></span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.15em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">O</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.3011em;"><span style="top: -2.55em; vertical-align: -0.15em;"></span><span class="mord">6</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">O</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.3011em;"><span style="top: -2.55em; margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mathrm mtight">2</span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.15em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">→</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8333em; vertical-align: -0.15em;"></span><span class="mord">6</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">C</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">O</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.3011em;"><span style="top: -2.55em; margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mathrm mtight">2</span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.15em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span><span class="mbin">+</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8333em; vertical-align: -0.15em;"></span><span class="mord">6</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">H</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.3011em;"><span style="top: -2.55em; margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mathrm mtight">2</span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.15em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mord mathrm">O</span></span></span></span></span></span></div>
<p>A positive assimilation reaction is usually indicated by the presence of growth. Several methods are available for performing the test. The method</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Medical Mycology</p>
<p>using carbohydrate impregnated disks is convenient and commonly used in clinical laboratories. Characteristic carbohydrate utilization profiles are used to distinguish one species from another, but not usually for differentiating genera.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="requirements">Requirements<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#requirements" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>A carbohydrate-free basal medium such as Yeast Nitrogen Base (YNB) agar.</li>
<li>Carbohydrate impregnated filter paper discs: Soak sterile filter-paper disks, 10 mm in diameter, in 1% carbohydrate solution and dry before use.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="technique">Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Prepare a suspension of the yeast to be identified in saline or distilled water to a density equivalent to McFarland Standard No. 4.</p>
<ul>
<li>Cover the surface of the YNB agar with the yeast cell suspension.
Aspirate the excess of suspension using a sterile pasteur pipette and allow the agar surface to dry for 5 minutes.</li>
<li>Using sterile forceps, place the discs of selected carbohydrates onto the surface of the agar, about 30 mm apart from each other.</li>
<li>Incubate the plate at 30°C for 24-48 hours.</li>
<li>Observe for the presence of growth around each disc which indicates that the particular carbohydrate has been assimilated by the yeast.</li>
<li>Interpret the results to identify the yeast as shown in Table 3.2.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="ii-fermentation-tests">(ii) Fermentation tests<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ii-fermentation-tests" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Alcoholic fermentation is the process by which a carbohydrate is fermented anaerobically by yeasts to produce ethanol and carbon dioxide. This fermentation is indicated by the production of gas.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="requirements">Requirements<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#requirements" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="yeast-fermentation-broth">Yeast fermentation broth<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#yeast-fermentation-broth" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Peptone</th><th>10 g</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>NaCl</td><td>5 g</td></tr><tr><td>Beef extract</td><td>3 g</td></tr><tr><td>Distilled water</td><td>1 litre</td></tr><tr><td>1N NaOH</td><td>1 ml.</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="indicator-solution-bromocresol-purple">Indicator solution (Bromocresol purple)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#indicator-solution-bromocresol-purple" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Bromocresol purple</th><th>0.04 g</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Distilled water</td><td>100 ml</td></tr><tr><td>1N NaOH</td><td>A few drops to make the solution alkaline.</td></tr></tbody></table>
<ul>
<li>Let the solution stand overnight for the dye to dissolve. Add 1N HCl till pH 7.0 is reached.</li>
<li>Add 100 ml indicator to 1 litre of fermentation broth.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="carbohydrate-stock-solutions">Carbohydrate stock solutions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#carbohydrate-stock-solutions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Dissolve 20 g of carbohydrate in 100 ml of distilled water by placing in a 56°C water bath for a few minutes.</li>
<li>Sterilize by steaming, or by filtration through 0.45 millipore filters.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="preparation-of-tubes">Preparation of tubes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#preparation-of-tubes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Pipette 9 ml of broth medium with indicator in a test tube containing a Durham tube and sterilize by autoclaving at 15 psi for 15 minutes.</li>
<li>Add 0.5 ml of stock carbohydrate solution to each broth tube just before use. Glucose, maltose, lactose and sucrose are most commonly used.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="technique">Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Prepare the yeast suspension in saline and add 0.2 ml of the suspension to each carbohydrate medium to be tested.</li>
<li>Incubate at 25-30°C for up to 24 days before discarding them as negative.</li>
</ul>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="iii-urea-hydrolysis">(iii) Urea hydrolysis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#iii-urea-hydrolysis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>A number of yeasts produce the enzyme urease which hydrolyses urea to form ammonia and carbon dioxide. This test is useful for distinguishing between the medically important yeasts of genera <em>Cryptococcus</em> and <em>Rhodotorula</em> (both urease positive) from those of genus <em>Torulopsis</em>, (urease negative). The test can also be used to identify species within the genera <em>Candida</em> and <em>Trichosporon</em>. (Table 3.2).</p>
<p><strong>Requirements</strong> Christensen's urea agar (refer to Bacteriology section).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="technique">Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Inoculate the yeast on the Christensen's urea agar slant.</li>
<li>Incubate the slant at 30°C for five days.</li>
<li>Pink or red colour of the agar indicates a positive test.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="commercial-yeast-identification-kits">Commercial Yeast Identification Kits<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#commercial-yeast-identification-kits" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Several packaged kits have been introduced by commercial companies. Most of the methods are rapid and results are available within 72 hours. These systems provide an identification based on a very broad database of several yeast biotypes. Examples of such systems are API-20C yeast system, API yeast-Ident system, Uni-yeast Tek system etc.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Chapter 4</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="serodiagnosis-of-mycotic-infections">Serodiagnosis of Mycotic Infections<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#serodiagnosis-of-mycotic-infections" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The isolation and identification of the aetiological agent from a clinical specimen is sufficient to confirm the diagnosis of a fungal infection. Occasionally, however, it may not be possible to isolate the fungus. In such cases, immunological and serological tests can provide an additional information which can lead to a diagnosis.</p>
<p>The specific immune response resulting from the exposure to the cell wall, cytoplasmic or extracellular fungal antigens during infections can be used for the diagnosis of mycotic infections. By monitoring this response, the prognosis and the result of therapy can also be assessed. The humoral antibody production or the presence of fungal antigens in the body fluids can be detected by serological methods. In vivo skin tests and in vitro lymphocyte stimulation tests are used to detect changes in cell-mediated immunity. The type of immune response depends on the fungus involved, the form of the disease and the immunological competence of the host.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="tests-based-on-cell-mediated-immunity-cmi">TESTS BASED ON CELL MEDIATED IMMUNITY (CMI)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tests-based-on-cell-mediated-immunity-cmi" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-skin-tests">1. Skin Tests<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-skin-tests" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Persons who are infected with fungi such as <em>Histoplasma</em> or <em>Coccidioides</em> develop a delayed type IV hypersensitivity reaction within one to fourteen days of infection. The sensitisation persists for many years and can be demonstrated by a skin test. If a fungal extract or a culture filtrate of the fungus is inoculated intradermally into the patient, the sensitisation of the patient is indicated by the appearance of induration (swelling) and erythema (redness) at the site of injection within 24 to 72 hours.</p>
<p><strong>Limitations of the skin tests</strong> In areas where a particular fungal infection is endemic, a large proportion of the population may show a positive skin test due to previous exposure to the sensitising fungal antigen. In such cases, the skin test is more useful for epidemiological work than for diagnosis.</p>
<p>Because of the complex nature of the fungal antigens and the sharing of antigens by some fungi, cross-reactions can occur. This can be overcome by testing with several fungal antigens simultaneously so that the relative strength of reaction to each antigen can be compared.</p>
<p>Moreover, a false negative skin test can be obtained if the infection is at an early stage or when the cellular immunity is impaired.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="2-in-vitro-tests-for-cmi">2. In Vitro Tests for CMI<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-in-vitro-tests-for-cmi" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Acquired hypersensitivity can be demonstrated in vitro by tests such as macrophage migration inhibition.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1080 Medical Mycology</p>
<p>bition or blast formation of sensitised lymphocytes in the presence of specific fungal antigens.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="tests-based-on-humoral-antibody-response">TESTS BASED ON HUMORAL ANTIBODY RESPONSE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tests-based-on-humoral-antibody-response" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-detection-of-antibodies">1. Detection of antibodies<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-detection-of-antibodies" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The techniques used for the detection of antibodies and antigens are essentially the same as those commonly used for other microorganisms. The tests detect antibodies belonging to IgG, IgM and IgA subclasses of immunoglobulins. The predominant class of antibody produced depends on the host, the fungus and the type and stage of infection.</p>
<p><strong>Limitations of antibody detection tests</strong> The use of serological tests for antibody detection is limited only to the diagnosis of systemic and subcutaneous infections because there is minimal or no humoral response in superficial mycoses.</p>
<p>For some diseases, there may be cross-reactions due to the complex and crude nature of antigenic extracts, which may make the interpretation and diagnosis very difficult. Unfortunately, this problem is most acute in opportunistic systemic mycoses which are very difficult to diagnose by other means. Testing of paired sera is useful to detect a rise in titre. At least two to four-fold rise in titre indicates a current infection.</p>
<p>Table 4.1 shows various immunological and serological tests commonly used in the diagnosis of fungal infections.</p>
<p>Antibodies of the IgM class can be detected about two weeks after the infection. If detected, their presence indicates a recent infection. Usually these antibodies are not detectable after six months of infection. IgM antibodies can be detected by precipitation, agglutination or immunodiffusion methods. On the other hand IgG antibodies appear shortly after the rise in IgM titre and may remain in high titre from 6-12 weeks to many months after infection. A single high titre of IgG antibodies, therefore, does not necessarily mean a current infection. IgG antibodies can be detected by tests such as complement fixation or immunodiffusion.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="2-detection-of-antigens">2. Detection of Antigens<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-detection-of-antigens" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Tests for the detection of fungal antigens are particularly useful in the early stages of the disease and in patients with impaired immunity when tests for antibody detection are of limited value. For example, the latex agglutination test for the detection of capsular antigen in cerebrospinal fluid is used to establish early diagnosis of cryptococcal infection.</p>
<p>Antigen detection tests are available for the detection of antigens of <em>Aspergillus</em> and <em>Candida</em> species. The tests need to be very sensitive to be able to detect the low levels of circulating antigens.</p>
<p>Commercial kits are also available for serological tests for some fungal diseases.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Serodiagnosis of Mycotic Infections 1081</p>
