<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="fungal-infections">Fungal Infections<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fungal-infections" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Fungal infections of man can be classified on the basis of the body site affected. They are broadly divided into four groups:</p>
<p>I. Superficial mycoses
II. Subcutaneous mycoses
III. Systemic or deep seated mycoses
IV. Opportunistic mycoses</p>
<p>These infections can be caused by filamentous fungi, dimorphic fungi or yeasts, irrespective of the body site affected, as shown in Table 1.1.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="i-superficial-mycoses">I. SUPERFICIAL MYCOSES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-superficial-mycoses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Superficial infections are of two types: surface infections and cutaneous infections.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="a-surface-infections">A. Surface Infections<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-surface-infections" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Fungi that cause surface infections live exclusively on the dead layers of the skin and its appendages. They have no contact with the living tissue and therefore, induce no immune response. The infections produced are asymptomatic. They produce aesthetically displeasing lesions showing hypopigmentation or hyperpigmentation of the skin; or nodules on the hair shaft away from the skin.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-tinea-versicolor-or-pityriasis-versicolor">1. Tinea Versicolor or Pityriasis Versicolor<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-tinea-versicolor-or-pityriasis-versicolor" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Causative agent</strong> <em>Malassezia furfur</em></p>
<p><strong>Clinical disease</strong> Tinea versicolor is a mild to chronic infection of the outer dead layer of the skin, the stratum corneum. It is usually asymptomatic. The lesions are discrete or confluent scaly areas which appear discoloured or depigmented. They occur on the smooth surfaces of the body such as the chest, abdomen, back and upper limbs.</p>
<p><strong>Laboratory diagnosis</strong></p>
<p><strong>Specimen</strong> Skin scrapings from the lesion.</p>
<p><strong>Techniques</strong></p>
<p><strong>Direct KOH mount</strong> The fungus appears as clusters of bottle-shaped yeast cells with short, branched hyphae.</p>
<p><strong>Culture</strong> This yeast does not grow on Sabouraud's agar but Sabouraud's agar covered with olive oil can be used for its isolation because of its lipophilic nature. Direct KOH mount in association with clinical symptoms establishes the diagnosis.</p>
<p>&gt; <strong>Note</strong>
&gt; M. furfur has been found to cause disseminated infections in infants and children who are on lipid replacement therapy. Blood and skin culture shows</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Fungal Infections 1083</p>
<p>the typical bottle shaped yeast cells without hyphae.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-tinea-nigra">2. Tinea Nigra<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-tinea-nigra" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Causative agent Exophiala werneckii (formerly, Cladosporium werneckii).</p>
<p>Clinical disease Tinea nigra is a localised infection, particularly of the hands and soles of the feet. It produces brownish or black coloured patches.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Specimen Skin scrapings</p>
<p>Techniques</p>
<p>Direct KOH mount The KOH mount of skin scrapings shows brownish, branched budding cells and septate hyphae.</p>
<p>Culture Exophiala werneckii is a dematiaceous fungus, appearing as black, shiny, yeast-like colonies on Sabouraud's agar, and later becoming filamentous and velvety-grey. Microscopically, it shows olive coloured budding yeast cells: which later may show one or two celled conidia arising from hyphae.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-piedra">3. Piedra<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-piedra" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Causative agents Two varieties of piedra have been recognized; Black piedra is caused by Piedraia hortae and white piedra is caused by Trichosporon beigelii.</p>
<p>Clinical disease Piedra is a fungal infection of the hair, mainly of the scalp, characterised by the appearance of firm, irregular nodules along the hair shaft. The nodules are composed of fungal elements cemented together on the hair. The nodule produced by Piedraia hortae is black in colour and is more common than the white nodule produced by Trichosporon beigelii.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Specimen Nodule on the hair shaft.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="technique">Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Direct KOH mount Mount the nodule in 10% KOH, place a coverslip and apply slight pressure to crush the nodule. Observe microscopically.</p>
<p>Black nodule shows oval asci approximately 20-50 µm × 5 µm in size, containing 2 to 8 aseptate ascospores of Piedraia hortae. White nodule, formed by a yeast-like fungus Trichosporon beigelii, shows hyaline hyphae 2-4 µm in diameter, and arthroconidia. The hyphae are bound together by a cement-like substance.</p>
<p>Culture Both the causative agents grow on Sabouraud's agar, though P. hortae is inhibited by cycloheximide.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="b-cutaneous-infections-dermatophytoses-or-dermatomycoses">B. Cutaneous Infections (Dermatophytoses or Dermatomycoses)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#b-cutaneous-infections-dermatophytoses-or-dermatomycoses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Dermatomycoses are fungal infections that involve only superficial keratinized tissues of the body: skin, hair and nails. These cutaneous mycoses are the most common fungal infections of man and are popularly called tinea or ringworm. Cutaneous infections caused by Candida albicans are also included in this group.</p>
<p>Causative agents Causative agents of dermatomycoses are collectively called dermatophytoses. About 40 species of dermato-phytes are known to cause infections in man and animals. They belong to three genera: Trichophyton, Microsporum and Epidermophyton. The genus Trichophyton is capable of invading the hair, skin and nails. the genus Microsporum involves the hair and skin; and the genus Epidermophyton, the skin and nails.</p>
<p>Clinical disease Dermatophytes grow only in the keratinised layers of the skin and its appendages. They utilise keratin as a source of nitrogen and are usually incapable of penetrating into the deeper, living tissue.</p>
<p>The lesions caused by dermatophytes are referred to as tinea. In gross appearance, the lesion shows an outer ring of an active, progressive infec</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Medical Mycology</p>

<ul>
<li>May be associated with lesions of hand and nails.</li>
</ul>
<p>tion with a central healing region. The body sites affected include scalp and hair (tinea capitis), beard (tenia barbae), body (tinea corporis), food (tinea pedis) and nail (tinea unguinum). Table 5.1 shows features of some ringworm infections and their causative agents.</p>
<p>The ringworm infections have a world-wide distribution. The infections can be acquired from:</p>
<p>(i) man to man (anthropophilic) e.g. Trichophyton rubrum, Microsporum audouinii or Epidermophyton floccosum.
(ii) Animal to man (zoophilic) e.g. Microsporum canis, Trichophyton verrucosum.
(iii) Soil to man (geophilic) e.g. Microsporum gypseum, Trichophyton ajelloi.</p>
<p><strong>Note</strong></p>
<p>In the lesions of the skin, all dermatophytes appear as branching septate hyphae with or without authrospores (Fig. 2.1 an Table 2.3). The identification of the species is possible only by culture.</p>
<p>Laboratory diagnosis Laboratory diagnosis of dermatophytoses is made by microscopic and cultural examination of the specimen such as skin, nail or hair. The identification of the isolate depends on the colonial morphology and microscopic appearance of the isolated fungus. Some other special techniques may be used for identification if necessary.</p>
<p>Specimens Skin scrapings, nail scrapings or fragments, hair.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Fungal Infections 1085</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="techniques">Techniques<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Direct KOH mount In the KOH mount of a properly digested skin scraping or nail fragments, the fungus appears as hyaline septate hyphae of uniform width (3-15 μm) which may be associated with chains of arthroconidia (Fig. 2.1, Table 2.3).</p>
<p>The KOH mount of infected hairs may show two types of infections. The infection may be ectothrix i.e. the arthroconidia appear on the periphery of the hair shaft, or endothrix, when the arthroconidia are seen within the hair shaft (Fig. 3.1). Trichophyton schoenleinii can cause endothrix infection but does not break up into arthroconidia. This type of endothrix infection is called favus. Some examples of fungi causing ectothrix and endothrix infections are shown in Table 5.2.</p>
<p>Culture Inoculate the specimen on Sabouraud's agar plates with and without cyclohexinide and chloramphenicol. Incubate the cultures at 22-26°C, examining every 2-3 days, for up to 4-6 weeks.</p>
<p>Identify the isolated fungus by colonial and microscopic morphology (lactophenol cotton blue preparation). The dermatophytes most commonly isolated from clinical specimens include Trichophyton rubrum, T. mentagrophytes, T. tonsurans, T. verrucossum, T. violaceum, T. schoenleinii, Microsporum canis, M. audouinii, M. gypseum and Epidermophyton floccosum. Table 5.3 describes characteristics of some dermatophytes.</p>
<p>A simple scheme for their identification based on microscopic appearance is shown in Fig 5.1. Colonial morphology of the fungus, that is the texture, colour, pigmentation, folds and growth rate should be observed. The lactophenol cotton blue preparation should be examined microscopically for the morphology of microconidia and macroconidia; for special structures such as chlamydospores; and for special hyphal characters such as spiral hyphae (T. mentagrophytes), antler or favic chandier hyphae (T. schoenlinii, T. violaceum), thickened, distorted (pectinate) hyphae (M. audouinii).</p>
<p>General characteristics of some common species of dermatophytes (Fig. 5.2) are described below:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="genus-trichophyton">Genus Trichophyton<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#genus-trichophyton" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Microscopically, the genus Trichophyton is characterised by smooth, club-shaped macroconidia borne singly at the terminal ends of hyphae or on short conidiophores. The macroconidia are thin walled with 8-10 septa, range in size from 4 × 8 to 8 × 15 μm and are usually few in number. The microconidia predominate. They are 2-4 μm in size and may be spherical, clavate (club shaped) or pyriform (teardrop shaped).</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Microscopic appearance</th><th>Dermatophyte</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Ectothrix infection</td><td>M. audouinii, M. canis, M. ferrugineum</td></tr><tr><td>2-5μm spores, massed</td><td>T. mentagrophytes</td></tr><tr><td>2-5μm spores, in chains</td><td>M. gypseum</td></tr><tr><td>5-8μm spores, massed or chains</td><td>T. verrucosum</td></tr><tr><td>8-10μm spores, in chains</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Endothrix infection</td><td>T. soudanese, T. tonsurans, T. violaceum</td></tr><tr><td>4-8μm spores in chains</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Favic Infection</td><td>T. schoenleinii</td></tr><tr><td>No spores, only hyphae</td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>

flat; powdery to granular or fluffy; reverse light tan, yellow, red or reddish-brown; sometimes producing a diffusible melanoid pigment</td><td>Round to pyriform microconidia in clusters or singly along the hyphae; clavate macroconidia present in some strains; coiled hyphae (spirals) are usually seen.</td><td>Urease positive; perforates hair in vitro; grows at 37°C.</td></tr></tbody></table>

<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-c00c7215.jpeg" alt="img-27.jpeg">
Microsporum audouinii</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-bddb3614.jpeg" alt="img-28.jpeg">
Epidermophyton floccosum</p>
<p>Fig. 5.2 Microscopic appearance of various species of dermatophytes</p>
<p>Trichophyton rubrum is a slow growing fungus and produces white to reddish colony with a cottony or velvety surface. The characteristic cherry-red colour, which gives the fungus its name 'rubrum' (Latin word for red), appears after 2-3 weeks on the reverse side of the colony.</p>
<p>Microscopically, macroconidia are not very commonly seen. When present, they are pencil shaped, thin walled with 3-8 septa. Microconidia are generally abundant, borne laterally on hyphae and appear as teardrops. T. rubrum is a very common cause of skin, nail and hair infections.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1090 Medical Mycology</p>
<p>T.mentagrophytes appears in two distinct varieties: granular or fluffy. The granular colonies sporulate more freely with numerous small, spherical microconidia in grape-like clusters (Fig. 5.2). Cigar shaped macroconidia may be present. Spiral hyphae are seen in some isolates.</p>
<p>T.mentagrophytes perforates hair and produces urease within 2–3 days of incubation. These features are used to differentiate it from T.rubrum, because T.mentagrophytes also is frequently isolated from skin, hair and nail infections.</p>
<p>T.tonsurans is the commonest cause of tinea capitis affecting the scalp and hair. It produces an endothrix infection. The colonial and microscopic morphology is described in Table 5.3. The microconidia vary greatly in shape and size. 'Balloon form' microconidia and chlamydospores are seen in old cultures.</p>
<p>T.verrucosum is a zoophilic fungus. The infections are acquired by man from cattle. It mainly affects the beard, neck, wrist and back of hands. Colonial and microscopic morphology is described in Table 5.3. Chlamydospores in chains and branching hyphae may be the only characteristic structures observed microscopically.</p>
<p>T.schoenleinii causes a severe type of infection of the scalp called favus. It produces cup-shaped crusts with considerable scarring of the scalp, sometimes leading to alopecia (loss of hair). Microscopically, the hair infection is typical. It shows arthroconidia at the base of the hair follicle and branching hyphae (favus) throughout the length of the hair shaft (Fig. 3.1c).</p>
<p>Longitudinal tunnels or empty spaces in the hair shaft indicate disintegrated hyphae. Cultural and microscopic characteristics of the fungus are described in Table 5.3. Short lateral and terminal branches, which become knobbly and club-shaped at the terminal ends, are described as antler hyphae or favic chandeliers and are characteristic. Chlamydospores are generally numerous.</p>
<p>T.violaceum causes infection of the scalp and the body. The hair infection is endothrix showing chains of arthroconidia in the hair shaft. In the laboratory it grows very slowly. Macroconidia and microconidia are rarely seen. Distorted hyphae and chlamydospores are a common feature (Fig.5.2).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="genus-microsporum">Genus Microsporum<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#genus-microsporum" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The presence of large (10–30 μm × 40–120 μm), spindle-shaped, thick, rough-walled (upto 4 μm) macroconidia, containing 4 to 15 septa, is characteristic of most species of the genus Microsporum. Microconidia are generally scanty. When present they are small (3–7 μm), club-shaped (clavate) and are borne laterally, either directly or with short conidiophores, on the hyphae.</p>
<p>M.canis is a zoophilic fungus. Humans acquire infections through contact with infected animals, particularly cats and dogs. Infections of the body (tinea corporis) and scalp (tinea capitis) are most common. The hair infection is of the ectothrix type. M. canis grows rapidly on laboratory media. Abundant, large (15 μm × 60–120 μm), thick walled, spindle shaped, multi-celled (4–8) macroconidia with curved ends are characteristic of the species. Microconidia are usually few in number.</p>
<p>M.audouinii is one of the most common causes of tinea capitis especially among school children. It is an anthropophilic fungus. Transmission of the disease is from man to man, and through fomites such as caps, combs and upholstery etc. The hair infection is of the ectothrix type. M. audouinii grows more slowly than other species of Microsporum and does not usually sporulate in culture. Microscopically, chlamydospores, and thickened, distorted (pectinate) hyphae are seen. Characteristic macroconidia of the genus are generally absent.</p>
<p>M. gypseum is a geophilic fungus, free-living in soil. It occasionally causes human infections. The hair infection is of the ectothrix type, the arthroconidia being much larger in size than others. M. gypseum grows rapidly on laboratory media. Abundant, large, multi-celled (4–9) macroconidia are seen. They differ from those of M. canis in shape. Although spindle shaped,</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Fungal Infections 1091</p>
<p>macroconidia of <em>M. gypseum</em> are more rounded at the distal end than those of <em>M. canis</em>.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="genus-epidermophyton">Genus Epidermophyton<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#genus-epidermophyton" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This genus includes only one species, <em>E. floccosum</em>. It can cause infections of the skin and nails, and is a common cause of tinea cruris (groins) and tinea pedis. It is seen as fine branching hyphae in KOH mounts of skin or nail scrapings.</p>
<p><em>E. floccosum</em> grows slowly on laboratory media. Microscopically, a large number of macroconidia, borne singly or in clusters of two or three on a conidiophore is seen. They are thin walled, club-shaped and may contain one to five cells. Microconidia are always absent. Chlamydospores are usually numerous.</p>
<p>&gt; <strong>Note</strong>
&gt; <em>E. floccuosum is susceptible to low temperatures. Therefore, the specimen should never be refrigerated before culture.</em></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="ii-subcutaneous-mycoses">II. SUBCUTANEOUS MYCOSES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ii-subcutaneous-mycoses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Subcutaneous mycoses are infections that involve the skin and subcutaneous tissue. Generally, there is no dissemination to other organs of the body. However, there are exceptions to the rule; <em>Sporothrix schenckii</em> may affect the lungs, meninges, joints, and other organs. Fungi causing subcutaneous mycoses grow in soil or on decaying vegetation. It is a very diverse group of fungi which belong to several unrelated fungal genera. Man and animals act as accidental hosts. The fungal spores are introduced in the cutaneous or subcutaneous tissue through a trauma such as a thorn prick or contamination of an open wound by soil.</p>
<p>&gt; <strong>Note</strong>
&gt; <em>Being a very diverse group, only the fungi most commonly isolated from subcutaneous mycoses are discussed here.</em></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-mycetoma-madurmycosis-or-madura-foot">1. Mycetoma (Madurmycosis or Madura Foot)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-mycetoma-madurmycosis-or-madura-foot" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Mycetoma was first reported from Madura, a town in Southern India, and therefore, is commonly known as Madurmycosis or Madura foot. It is a chronic, slowly progressive, granulomatous infection of the subcutaneous tissue, usually of the foot, and rarely, of the other parts of the body.</p>
<p><strong>Causative agents</strong> Mycetoma may be caused by a number of actinomycetes and filamentous fungi. A similar condition, botryomycosis is caused by pyogenic bacteria such as <em>Staphylococcus aureus</em>. Identification of the causative agent of mycetoma is therefore, extremely important for treatment.</p>
<p>Fungi commonly involved in mycetoma are <em>Madurella mycetomatis</em>, <em>M. grisea</em>, <em>Phialophora jeanselmei</em>, <em>Leptosphaeria senegalensis</em>, <em>Petriellidium</em> (<em>Allescheria</em>) <em>boydii</em>, <em>Acremonium</em> species. Mycetoma is also caused by actinomycetes such as: <em>Actinomyces madurae</em>, <em>A. pelletieri</em>, <em>Streptomyces somaliensis</em>, <em>Nocardia brasiliensis</em>.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-disease">Clinical Disease<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>After the entry of the organism into the tissue, the disease usually begins as a small subcutaneous swelling of the foot. It enlarges, burrows into the deeper tissues and finally tracks to the skin surface as multiple sinuses discharging viscid, seropurulent fluid containing granules. The granules are microcolonies of the causative agent. Macroscopic and microscopic examination of the granules is of diagnostic value.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Specimen</strong> Exudate from the mycetoma, or tissue.</p>
<ol>
<li>Macroscopic examination Examine the specimen for the presence of granules. The colour, size and consistency of the granules are helpful in the identification of the causative agent (Table 5.4).</li>
</ol>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1092 Medical Mycology</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-microscopic-examination">2. Microscopic examination<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-microscopic-examination" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Direct KOH mount</strong> Crush the granules in a drop of 10% KOH between two slides and examine microscopically. Observe the presence of branching, intertwining hyphae. If they are 1μm or less in diameter, they belong to the group of actinomycetes and the infection is called actinomycetoma. If the hyphae are 4 to 8 μm in diameter, the causative agent is a filamentous fungus, and the infection is called eumycetoma.</p>
<p>If it is an actinomycetoma, prepare a smear, air dry and stain by Gram's method (refer to Bacteriology section) and confirm the morphology of Gram positive filamentous bacteria. Identify by bacteriological methods.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-culture">3. Culture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-culture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Inoculate the specimen on Sabourand's agar, with and without cycloheximide and chloramphenicol; and brain heart infusion agar. Incubate at 22–26°C and examine after 2–3 days for four weeks.</p>
<p>If there is no growth discard the cultures as negative after four weeks. Identify the fungi isolated on the basis of colonial morphology, microscopic morphology and growth rate. Use other tests if necessary.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-chromoblastomycosis">2. Chromoblastomycosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-chromoblastomycosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Causative agents</strong> The disease is widely distributed in the tropical and subtropical areas of the world. Chromoblasto-mycosis is caused by a group of geophilic dematiaceous (dark coloured) fungi. Three genera are known to cause the disease: <em>Cladosporium</em>, e.g. <em>Cladosporium carrionii</em>, <em>Phialophora</em>, e.g. <em>Phialophora verrucosa</em>, and <em>Fonsecaea</em>, e.g. <em>Fonsecaea pedrosoi</em>.</p>
<p><strong>Clinical disease</strong> Chromoblastomycosis occurs among agricultural workers who fail to wear protective clothing and footware. The spores of the geophilic fungi inhabiting the soil enter the subcutaneous tissue through thorn-pricks or splinter puncture wounds. The infection develops as a papule at the site of inoculation and form warty cutaneous nodules, resembling the florets of a cauliflower. The disease is usually confined to the subcutaneous tissues of the arms and legs, but may also involve the head, face, neck and other body surfaces. Occasionally, these fungi may cause brain abscess.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Specimen</strong> Tissue from the lesion.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="techniques">Techniques<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="i-direct-microscopy">(i) Direct microscopy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-direct-microscopy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p><strong>KOH Mount</strong> Prepare a KOH mount.</p>
<p>Examine microscopically for <em>sclerotic bodies</em> (Fig. 5.3) which appear as round or irregular, dark brown, yeast like bodies with septa. They are about 5 to 20 μm in size.</p>
<p><strong>Tissue section</strong> stained by H&amp;E: (refer to Histopathology section).</p>
<p>Presence of <em>sclerotic bodies</em> in the section indicates chromoblastomycosis.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="ii-culture">(ii) Culture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ii-culture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Homogenize the tissue and inoculate Sabouraud's agar, with and without cycloheximide and chloromphenicol, and yeast extract phosphate medium if <em>Histoplasma capsulatum</em> is suspected.</p>
<p>Incubate the cultures at 30°C, examining every 2–3 days, for up to four weeks.</p>
<p>Identify the fungi isolated on the basis of colonial and microscopic morphology and other tests.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-fd7fd929.jpeg" alt="img-29.jpeg">
Fig. 5.3 <em>Sclerotic bodies in chromoblastomycosis</em></p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>

<p>Medical Mycology</p>
<p>Colonial morphology Causative agents of chromoblastomycosis produce darkly pigmented, slightly folded and heaped-up colonies with a greyish-velvetty surface, and showing a black pigment on the reverse.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="microscopic-morphology">Microscopic morphology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#microscopic-morphology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Cladosporium species Branching long chains of elliptical dematiaceous conidia (2–3 μm × 4–5 μm), arising from an erect, tall, branching conidiophore are characteristic of some common species of Cladosporium, e.g. Cladosporium carrionii.</p>
<p>Phialophora species It shows tube-like or cup-shaped phialides from which conidia arise, each having a distinct, small, collar-like projection (collarette). Conidia occur in clusters near the tip of the phialide, e.g. Phialophora verrucosa.</p>
<p>Fonsecaea species Primary conidia give rise to secondary conidia (sympodial arrangement). This gives an appearance of short chains of primary and secondary conidia around an erect sympodial conidiophore. Cladosporium type of sporulation may also occur showing phialides with collarettes.</p>
<p>Figure 5.4 shows characteristics of some fungi that cause chromoblastomycosis.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-phaeohyphomycosis">3. Phaeohyphomycosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-phaeohyphomycosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Causative agents There are about 70 dematiaceous fungi which are known to cause phaeohyphomycosis. The most common ones are the species of the genera Exophiala, Bipolaris, Alterneria, Phialophora and Cuvularia.</p>
<p>Clinical disease The causative agents of phaeohyphomycosis are associated with soil, wood and decaying plants. The infection begins when the fungus is implanted subcutaneously. It may then develop into firm nodules or a cyst, draining abscesses or ulcers. Sometimes, lungs may be affected due to inhalation of spores.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Specimen Exudate or aspirate from a cyst, or tissue.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="techniques">Techniques<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Direct KOH mount: A direct KOH mount of the specimen shows short or elongated, usually dark coloured, hyphal elements, or distorted and swollen yeast-like cells; or a mixture of both. Most causative agents are dematiaceous fungi.</p>
<p>Culture Most routine laboratories are not equipped for the culture and identification of the causative agent of phaecohyphomycosis. The specimens are usually sent to a reference laboratory for processing.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-sporotrichosis">4. Sporotrichosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-sporotrichosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Causative agent Sporotrichosis is a subcutaneous infection caused by the dimorphic fungus Sporothrix schenckii. It is a chronic infection having a world-wide distribution. The natural habitat of the fungus is the living or dead vegetation. The infection is an occupational hazard for farmers, gardeners and miners.</p>
<p>Clinical disease Humans acquire the infection through trauma such as thorn pricks or other minor injuries, usually on the hand, arm or leg. The infection begins as a small, non-healing ulcer. It develops later as nodular lesions of the skin and subcutaneous tissue. The fungus spreads from the primary site through lymphatics, but usually, does not extend beyond the regional lymph nodes. The subcutaneous nodules eventually break down and ulcerate.</p>
<p>Very rarely, the infection may disseminate to lungs and other organs.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Specimens Exudate from open draining lesions or from unopened subcutaneous nodules, or tissue.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Fungal Infections 1095</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Colonial Morphology</th><th>Microscopic Examination</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>(i) Growth rate slow,</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>cononies velvety, hair-like or cottony, shades</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>of olive green or green</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>black. <p><strong>Fig. 5.4</strong> Cultural and microscopic features of some fungi causing chromoblastomycosis</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="techniques">Techniques<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>(i) Direct microscopy
<strong>KOH</strong> mount <em>Sporothrix schenckii</em> appears sparsely as isolated yeast cells, 3–5 μm in diameter, which are usually round in shape and sometimes cigar shaped. The yeast cells do not show budding. Occasionally the fungus shows ‘asteroid bodies’ which are composed of a central fungus cell with eosinophilic material radiating from it.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1096 Medical Mycology</p>
<p>The detection of the yeast forms of <em>S. schenckii</em> in the tissue or exudate is very difficult because they are present in small numbers. Periodic-acid Schiff (PAS) or Gomori's methanamine silver (GMS) stains are more sensitive than the KOH mount. For details of these staining methods, refer to the Histopathology section.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="ii-culture">(ii) Culture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ii-culture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>At 30°C Being a dimorphic fungus, <em>Sporothrix schenckii</em> grows as a filamentous mould on media such as Sabouraud's agar at 30°C. It grows rapidly within 3-5 days. The colonies are usually small, moist, white to cream coloured initially, they gradually turn dark brown, wrinkled and membranous. Microscopically, the lactophenol cotton blue preparation shows delicate hyphae, which are 1–2 μm in thickness, branching, and septate. They bear single celled conidia, 2–5 μm in diameter. The conidia are arranged sympodially, appearing as flower-like clusters at the tips of single conidiophores (Fig. 5.5). The conidia are often dark brown in colour.</p>
<p>At 37°C On enriched media such as brain heart infusion blood agar (BHIBA), at 37°C, the colonies of <em>S. schenckii</em> appear as soft, and creamy. Microscopically, spherical, oval or cigar-shaped budding cells are seen. The conversion from mould to yeast form occurs within one to five days.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-ce7ee01e.jpeg" alt="img-30.jpeg">
Fig. 5.5 <em>Sporothrix schenckii</em> mould form</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="5-rhinosporidiosis">5. Rhinosporidiosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-rhinosporidiosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p><strong>Causative agent</strong> The agent of rhinosporidiosis is called <em>Rhinosporidium seeberi</em>. The disease is more prevalent in India and Sri Lanka, but is also reported from USA.</p>
<p><strong>Clinical disease</strong> Rhinosporidiosis is a chronic, granulomatous infection of the mucous membranes of the face, mouth or eye, with the development of friable polyps.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p><strong>Specimen</strong> Tissue</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="technique">Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p><strong>(i) Direct microscopy</strong> Histological examination of the tissue section stained by haematoxylin and eosin (H&amp;E) method (refer to Histopathology section) reveals a lesion composed of a large number of spherules, embedded in a stroma of connective tissue. The spherules vary in size from 10μm to 200-300 μm. When mature, the spherule contains a large number of endospores, each about the size of a red blood cell (7μm). The endospores are released from the spherule through a pore in the wall.</p>
<p><strong>(ii) Culture</strong> <em>Rhinosporidium seeberi</em> does not grow on inanimate laboratory media. It has been cultured in mammalian epithelial cell tissue culture.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="iii-systemic-mycoses">III. SYSTEMIC MYCOSES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#iii-systemic-mycoses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Systemic mycoses may involve any internal organ or tissue of the body, including the lymph nodes, bone, subcutaneous tissues and skin. Systemic fungal infections are caused by several fungi. Traditionally, systemic mycoses have included the infections caused by a group of fungi which are taxonomically unrelated, but have one characteristic in common: they are all dimorphic fungi. Besides these 'classic' systemic mycoses, 'opportunistic' infections caused by other moulds can also be systemic, but these are discussed separately.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Fungal Infections 1097</p>
<p>The infections caused by yeasts and yeast-like fungi can be subcutaneous or systemic and are also discussed under opportunistic infections.</p>
<p>The systemic mycoses caused by dimorphic fungi are:</p>
<ol>
<li>Blastomycosis</li>
<li>Coccidioidomycosis</li>
<li>Paracoccidioidomycosis</li>
<li>Histoplasmosis</li>
</ol>
<p>These dimorphic fungi exist in nature as saprophytic filamentous moulds. This form is quite distinct from the parasitic, invasive tissue form. This transformation from mould to tissue form <em>in vivo</em> can also be initiated <em>in vitro</em> in the laboratory when cultured on enriched media and incubated at 35–37°C.</p>
<p>Systemic mycoses vary greatly in severity, ranging from asymptomatic to fatal infections. Asymptomatic or minimally symptomatic diseases are usually self-limiting and may undergo spontaneous healing. Disseminated or progressive and potentially fatal infection is fairly uncommon. A disseminated disease can involve any organ, sometimes with symptoms characteristic of the infecting fungus. Systemic mycoses are more common in individuals with diminished immunological functions.</p>
<p>&gt; <strong>Note</strong>
&gt; If a dimorphic fungus is suspected, all the laboratory procedures should be performed in a Class II biological safety cabinet.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-blastomycosis">1. Blastomycosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-blastomycosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Causative agent</strong> Blastomycosis is a chronic infection caused by a dimorphic fungus, <em>Blastomyces dermatitidis</em>. It is primarily a pulmonary infection which may disseminate from an initial pulmonary focus to virtually any organ in the body.</p>
<p><em>B. dermatitidis</em> grows as budding yeast-like form in the host tissue and on enriched media at 35–37°C; and as a typical mould-like form at 25–30°C. Inhalation of either, the yeast-like forms or the arthroconidia of the mould-like form, can cause disease. The disease is very common in North America and therefore, is also called North American blastomycosis.</p>
<p><strong>Clinical disease</strong> Blastomycosis is more common in individuals who work with soil or wood. The infection is acquired by inhalation of the yeast form or arthrospores of the mould form. Primary infection resembles tuberculosis or histoplasmosis. The fungus may spread from the lungs to other parts of the body through the blood stream, and form multiple abscesses.</p>
<p>Primary cutaneous blastomycosis may be caused by inoculation of <em>B. dermatitidis</em> directly into the skin. The initial lesion is a papule, around which secondary nodules develop and later undergo ulceration.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="specimens">Specimens<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#specimens" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>(i) Sputum, collected on three consecutive days or at 2–3 days intervals.
(ii) Pus from involved organs.
(iii) Tissue biopsies.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="techniques">Techniques<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>(i) <strong>Direct KOH mount</strong> Microscopic examination of the KOH mount of the specimen shows large yeast cells, 8–15 μm in size, with a typical double-contoured, thick, refractile wall. Capsule is absent. Each yeast cell carries only a single bud, attached to the mother cell by a broad base (4–5 μm). They often remain attached till they have reached the size of the mother cell.</p>
<p>The tissue sections may be stained either by periodic acid Schiff (PAS) stain or Gomori’s methanamine silver (GMS) stain.</p>
<p>(ii) <strong>Culture</strong> Inoculate the specimen on Sabouraud’s agar and brain heart infusion blood agar (BHIBA). Incubate Sabouraud’s agar at 25–30°C and BHIBA at 37°C, for up to six weeks.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1098 Medical Mycology</p>
<p>Mould form At 30°C on Sabouraud's agar, the growth appears in the form of a mould in one to six weeks. The colonies may be white to tan in colour, glaborous (smooth) or cottony in texture with varying number of aerial hyphae.</p>
<p>Microscopically, the hyphae of the mould form are septate and delicate, measuring 1–2 μm in diameter. Round, smooth-walled conidia, 4–10 μm in diameter are produced at the end of slender conidiophores, resembling lollipops.</p>
<p>Figures 5.6 (a) and (b) show the mould and the yeast forms of <em>B. dermatitidis</em>.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-eb87a674.jpeg" alt="img-31.jpeg">
Fig. 5.6 Blastomyces dermatitidis</p>
<p>Yeast form Colonies of the yeast form develop within seven days and appear waxy and wrinkled, cream to tan in colour, on enriched media at 37°C. Microscopically, large, thick walled yeast cells with buds attached by a broad base (Fig. 5.6b) are seen.</p>
<p><strong>Note</strong> The yeast form may not be seen in the initial culture and several transfers may be necessary to achieve conversion. However, the appearance of a few broad based budding yeasts with residual hyphae can be identified as <em>B. dermatitidis</em>.</p>
<p>(iii) Exoantigen test The identification of <em>B. dermatidis</em> may be confirmed by the exoantigen test.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-coccidioidomycosis">2. Coccidioidomycosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-coccidioidomycosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Causative agent</strong> Coccidioidomycosis is caused by the dimorphic fungus <em>Coccidioides immitis</em>. It grows as a mould in the environment and at 30°C in the laboratory. The tissue form, a spherule containing a large number of endospores, develops <em>in vitro</em> only on special media.</p>
<p>Coccidioidomycosis is endemic in the dry, arid regions of South Western USA, where <em>C. immitis</em> is present in soil and in rodents.</p>
<p><strong>Clinical disease</strong> The infection is acquired by inhalation of the infective arthroconidia of <em>C. immitis</em>. About 60% of those infected remain asymptomatic. The majority of symptomatic primary infections are characterised by cough, fever and chest pain which subside in two to three weeks. The infection, however, may become disseminated, with extension to other organs: meninges, bones, lymph nodes, skin and subcutaneous tissues. Development of disseminated disease is more common among dark-skinned races.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="specimens">Specimens<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#specimens" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>(i) Sputum, collected on three consecutive days
(ii) Bronchial aspirate
(iii) Pus
(iv) CSF
(v) Lung or lymph node biopsy</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="techniques">Techniques<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="i-direct-microscopy">(i) Direct microscopy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-direct-microscopy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<p><strong>KOH Mount</strong> In the direct microscopic examination of KOH mount of the specimen, <em>Coccidioides immitis</em> occurs in the form of spherules ranging from 5–200 μm in diameter. When mature, the spherules have a thick wall and are filled with numerous round or irregularly shaped spores, called endospores (2–5 μm). A</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Fungal Infections 1099</p>
<p>mature spherule may contain several hundred endospores. When the sporulation is complete, the wall of the spherule ruptures and releases the endospores. These liberated endospores develop into mature spherules (Fig.5.7b).</p>
<p>Saline mount Sometimes, large mononuclear cells filled with granules resemble spherules of C. immitis. For confirmation, a wet preparation of the specimen is prepared using sterile saline, the edges of the coverglass are sealed with petroleum jelly or DPX and incubated overnight at 30°C. When spherules are present, multiple strands of hyphae protrude from the endospores.</p>
<p><strong>Note</strong> Hyphae or barrel-shaped arthroconidia with or without spherules may be seen in chronic pulmonary infections with C. immitis.</p>
<p>Culture Inoculate the specimen on Sabouraud's agar and blood agar which are incubated at 30°C, and on modified converse medium which is incubated at 40°C for up to three weeks.</p>
<p>Caution Because of its highly infectious nature, culture of C. immitis represents a biohazard to laboratory workers. Strict safety precautions must be followed when examining cultures. Use of petri dishes is not recommended. Test tube (25 × 150 mm) agar slants should be used. All manipulations should be performed in a safety cabinet.</p>
<p>Figures 5.7 (a) and (b) show the mould and the spherule forms of C immitis.</p>
<p>Mould form Colonies of C. immitis appear on Sabouraud's agar within three to five days, but may take up to three weeks. Some portions of the colony show aerial hyphae appearing fluffy, while other areas are smooth. The colour of the colonies ranges from pink to yellow in different isolates.</p>
<p>Microscopic examination of the colonies with lactophenol cotton blue shows small septate hyphae, which, in older cultures, break down to form rectangular or barrel-shaped darkly staining arthroconidia. The arthroconidia are separated from one another by clear or lighter staining non-viable or empty cells. These are referred to as alternate arthroconidia.</p>
<p>Spherule form In vitro conversion to spherule form occurs on modified converse medium when incubated at 40°C. However, it should be done only in reference laboratories to avoid the hazard of infection.</p>
<p>Exoantigen test It is highly sensitive and specific test for the identification of C. immitis.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-paracoccidioidomycosis-south-american-blastomycosis">3. Paracoccidioidomycosis (South American Blastomycosis)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-paracoccidioidomycosis-south-american-blastomycosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Causative agent The aetiological agent of paracoccidioidomycosis is Paracoccidioides brasiliensis. It is a dimorphic fungus which occurs as a yeast in tissues and on enriched media at 37°C; and as a mould at 25-30°C.</p>
<p>The infection is most commonly found in South America, especially in Brazil, Venezuela and Columbia, and less frequently in Mexico and Africa.</p>
<p>Clinical disease Paracoccidioidomycosis is acquired by inhalation of fungal elements from the environment. The exact source of infection is not known. The primary lung infection is generally asymptomatic and self healing. It may progress to symptomatic pulmonary or systemic disease. The most common sites are pulmonary, gastrointestinal and mucocutaneous regions. Ulcerative lesions occur most commonly on the face and in the oral mucosa. Dissemination to other sites, such as the lymphatic system, spleen, intestines, liver, brain, meninges and adrenal glands may occur in some patients.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="specimens">Specimens<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#specimens" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>(i) Sputum
(ii) Tissue scrapings
(iii) Exudate from lesions
(iv) Mucosal biopsy</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="techniques">Techniques<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="i-direct-microscopy">(i) Direct microscopy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-direct-microscopy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>KOH mount Microscopic examination of the KOH mount of the specimen shows typical large yeast forms, 10–30 µm in diameter, with multiple buds attached over the entire surface (Fig. 5.8). The mother cell with attached buds gives an appearance of a ‘mariner’s wheel’.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-de144504.jpeg" alt="img-33.jpeg">
Fig. 5.8 Paracoccidioides brasiliensis: Yeast form appearing as mariner’s wheel due to numerous buds</p>
<p>Biopsy specimens are stained by the standard histological techniques using periodic acid Schiff (PAS) or Gomori’s methanamine silver (GMS) stains.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="ii-culture">(ii) Culture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ii-culture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Inoculate the specimen on Sabouraud’s agar, incubate at 30°C; and on blood agar (BA) or brain heart infusion blood agar (BHIBA) at 37°C, for three to four weeks.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="mould-form">Mould form<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mould-form" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Colonies of <em>P. brasiliensis</em> require about three to four weeks to grow. Initially they are yeast-like and later are covered with aerial mycelium and become brownish in colour, often with a heaped surface.</p>
<p>On microscopic examination, <em>P. brasiliensis</em> appears in the form of fine septate hyphae. Formation of conidia in culture is not seen very often. When present, conidia are indistinguishable from those produced by <em>Blastocystis dermatitidis</em>.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="yeast-form">Yeast form<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#yeast-form" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>On blood enriched media at 37°C, <em>P. brasiliensis</em> grows faster than at 30°C. The colonies are smooth, cream to tan in colour. Microscopically, they show typical yeast cells with multiple budding.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="iii-exoantigen-test">(iii) Exoantigen test<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#iii-exoantigen-test" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>This test is necessary as a confirmatory test only if conversion to yeast form is unsuccessful.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
