<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="4-histoplasmosis">4. Histoplasmosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-histoplasmosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Causative agent The primary cause of histoplasmosis world wide, especially in the central and southern states of USA is <em>Histoplasma capsulatum</em>. Another species, <em>Histoplasma duboisii</em> is recognized as the agent of African histoplasmosis. Both fungi are intracellular parasites of the reticulo-endothelial system, and both are dimorphic. They grow as budding yeast cells in tissue or on specific enriched media at 37°C, while in the soil or on media incubated at room temperature, they exhibit the mould form.</p>
<p>Clinical disease The fungus is present in the soil, rotting trees and is abundant in bird faeces. Numerous cases of histoplasmosis have been reported in persons cleaning poultry farms. Infection is acquired by inhalation, but a large majority of cases are asymptomatic. Some of the infected persons develop pulmonary disease which resembles tuberculosis. Macroconidia of the mould form is the most likely infectious particle, which after reaching the lung converts into a yeast form and localises intracellularly in the monocytic phagocytes. Encapsulation, and eventually, calcification of the infected site occurs. Disseminated histoplasmosis develops only in a small minority of infected individuals, involving virtually any organ in the body through haematogenous spread. The reticuloendothelial system is most primarily involved with resultant hepatosplenomegaly, anaemia, fever and may lead to death. Granulomatous and ulcerative lesions may develop on the skin and mucosa.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="specimens">Specimens<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#specimens" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>(i) Sputum
(ii) Peripheral blood
(iii) Bone marrow
(iv) Urine
(v) Tissues from involved organs (e.g. skin)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="techniques">Techniques<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>(i) Direct microscopic examination Unstained preparation such as a KOH mount is usually unsatisfactory for the detection of <em>Histoplasma</em> yeast form in tissues. Preparations from the peripheral blood, bone marrow, urine deposit, impression smears of the tissue or scrapings from the mucosal lesions should be stained with Giemsa and examined microscopically.</p>
<p><em>H. capsulatum</em> appears as intracellular yeasts, 2–4 μm in diameter, as round bodies with a central stained area and a halo-like capsule surrounding it. They are present in monocytes, macrophages and sometimes in polymorphonuclear leukocytes.</p>
<p>Figure 5.9 a and b show the mould and the yeast forms of <em>H. capsulatum</em>.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-f0c8e983.jpeg" alt="img-34.jpeg">
Fig. 5.9 <em>Histoplasma capsulatum</em></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="note">Note<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#note" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>
<p>The yeast form of <em>H. capsulatum</em> resembles amastigote form of <em>Leishmania donovani</em> (see Parasitology section). Under oil immersion lens, <em>Leishmania donovani</em> shows the presence of a kinetoplast (a red stained body near the nucleus, that is absent in <em>H. capsulatum</em>).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><em>H. duboisii</em> has similar staining properties as <em>H. capsulatum</em>, but is larger in size (8–15 μm in diameter).</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Medical Mycology</p>
<ol start="3">
<li>H. duboisii should be differentiated from B. dermatitidis because they have a similar morphology except that the H. duboisii buds have a narrow attachment to the mother cell as opposed to the broad-based buds of B. dermatitidis.</li>
</ol>
<p>Tissue biopsies are stained by standard histological techniques using H and E, PAS or GMS staining methods.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="culture">Culture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#culture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Mould form</strong> For the mould form inoculate the specimen on Sabouraud's agar and incubate at 30°C for four weeks. Histoplasma grows very slowly as a mould at 30°C and usually requires two to four weeks. The growth begins as a white, cottony colony which turns brown to buff with age.</p>
<p>Microscopically, the hyphae are small (1–2 μm) forming rope-like strands. Large round macroconidia (8–14 μm) are seen in young cultures which later become roughened and show tubercle-like projections. These tuberculated macroconidia is the most distinctive feature of the mould form. Microconidia (2–4 μm), round or pyriform in shape, are also produced, which are regarded as the infectious particles.</p>
<p>H. capsulatum and H. duboisii are indistinguishable in the mould form. The mould form can be converted into the yeast form by inoculating it on enriched media and incubating at 37°C.</p>
<p><strong>Yeast form</strong> For the yeast form, inoculate the specimen on brain heart infusion blood agar or yeast extract phosphate medium and incubate at 37°C for four weeks. It is recommended that the growth should be successively subcultured at weekly intervals three to four times. Conversion from the mould form to the yeast form is difficult and requires several successive transfers at three to five days intervals.</p>
<p>Colonies of yeast are mucoid, membranous, white to tan in colour. Microscopically, a mixture of swollen hyphae and budding yeast cells, similar to those seen intracellularly in macrophages, are visible.</p>
<p><strong>Exoantigen test</strong> This test should be performed if a definitive identification is not reached on the basis of microscopy and culture.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="iv-opportunistic-mycoses">IV. OPPORTUNISTIC MYCOSES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#iv-opportunistic-mycoses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A group of saprophytic fungi, which are ubiquitous in nature and are common laboratory contaminants, can cause infections almost exclusively in immunocompromised patients. These opportunistic fungi can cause disease in patients who are compromised due to some underlying disease process, such as diabetes mellitus, leukaemia, lymphoma, or an impaired immune system or immunosuppressive therapy. Although harmless to normal healthy individuals, opportunistic fungi can produce serious and even fatal infections in debilitated persons.</p>
<p>Out of a large number of fungi which can cause opportunistic infections, only those infections which are frequently encountered will be discussed. They are:</p>
<ol>
<li>Aspergillosis</li>
<li>Zygomycosis</li>
<li>Cryptococcosis</li>
<li>Candidiasis (Candidosis, Moniliasis)</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-aspergillosis">1. Aspergillosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-aspergillosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Causative agents</strong> Aspergillosis can be caused by several species of <em>Aspergillus</em>. The commonest are <em>Aspergillus niger</em>, <em>A. fumigatus</em> and <em>A. flavus</em>.</p>
<p><strong>Clinical disease</strong> Aspergillus species are widespread in the environment, living mainly in soil and living plants. The spores are easily dispersed in the air and the majority of infections are acquired by inhalation of airborne spores.</p>
<p><strong>Aspergillosis</strong> includes various disease conditions such as toxicosis (due to aflatoxin produced by <em>A. flavus</em>); allergic reactions; localized infections of the skin, external ear (otomycosis), nasal sinuses and the orbit of the eye (keratitis); pulmonary infection; and disseminated multi-organ disease. Pulmonary aspergillosis may occur in the form of an allergy, aspergilloma or invasive tissue disease.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Fungal Infections 1103</p>
<p>Aspergilloma is the colonization by the fungus of the pre-existing cavities in the lung due to previous infections such as tuberculosis. In invasive aspergillosis, the mycelia extend into the tissue and subsequently may disseminate to other parts of the body through the blood stream.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="specimens">Specimens<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#specimens" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The specimens suitable for culture include:</p>
<p>(i) Sputum
(ii) Bronchial aspirate
(iii) Swabs or tissue biopsies from localized infections</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="techniques">Techniques<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="i-direct-microscopy">(i) Direct microscopy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-direct-microscopy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<p>KOH Mount On direct microscopic examination, species of Aspergillus shows dichotomous branching (at 45°) of hyaline, septate hyphae 3–4 μm in width (Fig. 5.10). The walls are parallel and refractile. However, on the basis of morphology, it is not possible to distinguish them from other septate fungi.</p>
<p>Tissue sections These are stained with standard histological techniques such as H and E staining.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-c212cfd8.jpeg" alt="img-35.jpeg">
Fig. 5.10 Hyphae of Aspergillus in sputum</p>
<p>The section shows poorly stained septate hyphae in a necrotic, pyogenic lesion.</p>
<p>(ii) Culture Inoculate the specimen on Sabouraud's agar without cycloheximide. Aspergillus is sensitive to cycloheximide. A medium enriched with blood may also be inoculated.</p>
<p>Aspergillus grows as a mould both at 30°C and 37°C rapidly within two to four days. Identification is based on both gross and microscopic morphology of the isolate, and pigment production. All species of Aspergillus produce erect unbranched conidiophores with swollen apices or vesicles. Depending on the species, a portion or the entire surface of the vesicle is covered by flask shaped phialides or sterigmata. Conidia are formed in chains from the tips of the phialides (Fig. 5.11a). The hyphae are septate.</p>
<p>Recently, about 200 species of the genus Aspergillus have been divided into 18 groups. A definitive identification to the species level should be requested from a reference laboratory. The most common groups are described below.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="aspergillus-fumigatus-group">Aspergillus Fumigatus Group<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#aspergillus-fumigatus-group" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The growth appears as a small white colony, becoming green or slate-grey and powdery with age. Microscopically, the phialides cover only upper half of the surface of the dome-shaped vesicle.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-37d6b67a.jpeg" alt="img-36.jpeg">
Fig. 5.11 (a) Characteristics of Aspergillus</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1104 Medical Mycology</p>
<p>Conidia are usually round and slightly rough, 2–3 μm in diameter. <em>A. fumigatus</em> is among a few species which are capable of growing at 45°C.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="aspergillus-flavus-group">Aspergillus Flavus Group<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#aspergillus-flavus-group" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><em>A. flavus</em> grows within one to five days, producing a yellow-green colony with a rough granular texture. Conidiophores are rough-walled, ending in a rounded vesicle. The phialides cover the entire surface of the vesicle and give rise to short chains of conidia.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="aspergillus-niger-group">Aspergillus Niger Group<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#aspergillus-niger-group" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The growth of <em>A. niger</em> appears within two to six days, beginning as a white filamentous growth which soon develops a black, dotted surface as conidia are produced. Microscopically, the long conidiophores terminate in spherical vesicles. The phialides cover the entire surface of the vesicle. The conidia are thick-walled, very rough and darkly pigmented showing carbon-black conidia.</p>
<p>&gt; <strong>Note</strong> Occasionally, non-sporulating isolates may be obtained from pulmonary lesions.</p>
<p>(iii) Exoantigen test It is useful for identifying non-sporulating isolates of <em>Aspergillus</em>.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-zygomycosis">2. Zygomycosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-zygomycosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Causative agents</strong> Zygomycosis is an opportunistic fungal infection caused by several genera within the class Zygomycetes. These moulds are characterized by broad, ribbon-like, non-septate hyphae. The spores are produced within a sac-like fruiting structure, a sporangium, which is formed at the tip of a supporting structure, a sporangiophore. Columnella is the protrusion of the sporangiophore into the sporangium. The spores are produced internally within the sporangium and are called sporangiospores. After maturation, the sporangium ruptures to release the spores into the environment. The sporangiophores are connected to each other by hyphae called stolons. The stolons, usually, remain in contact with the substrate by root-like structures, rhizoids. The three most common zygomycetes isolated in a clinical laboratory are species of <em>Rhizopus</em>, <em>Mucor</em> and <em>Abisidia</em>.</p>
<p><strong>Clinical disease</strong> Like many other opportunistic pathogens, the zygomycetes are abundant in the environment, and are transient residents of the human body. Throughout the world, the zygomycetes are known to cause infections in patients with predisposing conditions such as diabetes mellitus, malignancies and immunodeficiencies. The disease is acute and rapidly fatal, most often when associated with <em>Rhizopus</em> species. The most common form of the disease is the rhinocerebral form where the nasal mucosa, palate, sinuses, face, orbit and brain are involved. The other common forms are pulmonary and intestinal; and may disseminate to any organ of the body. Zygomycetes have a remarkable capacity to invade blood vessels. Small arteries can be filled with hyphae, which penetrate into the adjacent tissues and cut off the blood supply to the organ, leading to thrombosis and necrosis.</p>
<p>Cutaneous and subcutaneous zygomycosis is occasionally seen in burn cases and in post-operative wounds.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="specimens">Specimens<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#specimens" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>(i) Scrapings of the mucosal linings or necrotic discharge from the nose, sinuses, facial lesions or orbit of the eye.
(ii) Sputum, bronchial aspiration.
(iii) Biopsies of necrotic tissues from the edge of the lesions.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="techniques">Techniques<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="i-direct-microscopy">(i) Direct microscopy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-direct-microscopy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<p><strong>KOH mount</strong> The KOH mount of the specimen shows aseptate hyphae. The branching is not common. If present it is usually at 90°C.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Fungal Infections 1105</p>
<p>Report a positive finding as quickly as possible because zygomycosis can be rapidly fatal.</p>
<p>Tissue biopsies should be stained with standard histopathological techniques, preferably using special stains such as periodic acid Schiff (PAS) or Gomori's methanamine silver (GMS) stains.</p>
<p>(ii) Culture Inoculate the specimens on Sabouraud's agar without cycloheximide and chloramphenicol because zygomycetes are sensitive to them. Incubate the cultures at 30°C for two to four days, examining daily.</p>
<p>Zygomycete colonies are visible within 48–72 hours, and show characteristic morphological features. They produce abundant aerial mycelium with fluffy, white, grey or brown hyphal growth that rapidly covers the entire agar surface. It is not possible to distinguish between the genera on the basis of colony morphology. Microscopically, identification to the genus level is adequate. Microscopic features of the three important genera are described as follows.</p>
<p>Mucor species The sporangiophores are often branched. The sporangia are spherical. The distinctive feature of Mucor species is that the rhizoids are absent (Fig. 11b).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-f8e14941.jpeg" alt="img-37.jpeg">
Fig. 5.11 (b) Characteristics of Mucor Rhizopus species The sporangiophores are long and unbranched. The rhizoids and the sporangiophores arise at the same point where the</p>
<p>stolons touch the substrate. The sporangia are spherical (Fig. 11c).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-764c34a4.jpeg" alt="img-38.jpeg">
Fig. 5.11 (c) Characteristics of Rhizopus</p>
<p>Abisidia Species The rhizoids originate between the two sporangiophores. The sporangia are pear-shaped. The sporangiophores are branched and occur in groups of three to five.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-cryptococcosis">3. Cryptococcosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-cryptococcosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Causative agents Cryptococcosis is almost always caused by <em>Cryptococcus neoformans</em>. However, other species of the genus <em>Cryptococcus</em> are also occasionally associated with the disease.</p>
<p>Members of the genus <em>Cryptococcus</em> are true yeasts, which are unicellular fungi that reproduce asexually by budding. <em>Cryptococcus neoformans</em> exists as a saprophyte in nature. It is most often found associated with pigeon faeces. A distinctive feature of <em>C. neoformans</em> is that it produces a prominent polysaccharide capsule.</p>
<p>Clinical disease Cryptococcosis is acquired by inhalation of yeast through aerosols from the soil contaminated with bird excreta. The primary focus of infection is the lungs and it is often asymptomatic or may lead to mild pneumonia. As there is no calcification, healed pulmonary lesions may not be seen radiologically. Extra pulmonary disease is</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>1106 Medical Mycology</p>
<p>frequently observed in immunocompromised patients, particularly those with AIDS, Hodgkin's disease or leukaemia. Disseminated cryptococcos is most commonly involves the central nervous system causing meningitis, but can also cause endocarditis, hepatitis, renal infection and pleural effusion.</p>
<p>Antigenically, the polysaccharide capsule of <em>C. neoformans</em> can be differentiated into four serotypes, A, B, C, and D; A and D being common in temperate climate, while B and C prevail in tropical areas.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="specimens">Specimens<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#specimens" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>(i) Sputum or bronchial aspirate
(ii) CSF
(iii) Blood
(iv) Urine
(v) Tissue biopsies</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="techniques">Techniques<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="i-direct-microscopy">(i) Direct Microscopy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-direct-microscopy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<p><strong>KOH Mount</strong> A KOH mount of the specimen shows spherical, thick walled yeast cells, with single or multiple budding, varying in size from 2 to 15 μm.</p>
<p><strong>India Ink Preparation</strong> Negative staining with India ink shows a wide, refractile, polysaccharide capsule (Fig. 2.2). However, some strains of <em>C. neoformans</em> may not exhibit a capsule.</p>
<p>(ii) <strong>Culture</strong> Inoculate the specimen on Sabouraud's agar without cycloheximide and on blood agar. Incubate at 30°C for one week. It is recommended that for specimens such as CSF or urine, a 0.45 μm pore size membrane filter or centrifugation should be used for concentration of the specimen.</p>
<p>Colonies of <em>C. neoformans</em> appear within one to five days as smooth white to tan colonies which may become mucoid. The polysaccharide capsule may not be produced on culture media.</p>
<p>Microscopic examination of the colonies may provide a tentative identification of <em>C. neoformans</em> appearing as spherical yeast cells showing a wide variation in size. For final identification, biochemical tests may be performed as shown in Table 3.2.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-candidiasis-candidosis-moniliasis">4. Candidiasis (Candidosis, Moniliasis)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-candidiasis-candidosis-moniliasis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Causative agents</strong> Candidiasis is caused by yeast-like fungi belonging to the genus <em>Candida</em>. <em>C. albicans</em> is the most common causative agent followed by <em>C. tropicalis</em> and <em>Torulopsis glabrata</em>. <em>Candida</em> species form part of the normal flora of the skin, mucous membranes and intestinal tract.</p>
<p><strong>Clinical disease</strong> Candidiasis is an opportunistic endogenous infection. Depending on the immunocompetence of the host, candidiasis can range from trivial or mild infection of skin or mucosa to a very severe systemic disease (Table 5.5). The extent of system involvement appears to be directly related to the severity of the underlying disorder such as diabetes mellitus, leukaemia, lymphoma or T-cell deficiencies.</p>
<p>Cutaneous candidiasis may involve the skin folds between the fingers (intertriginous) or surrounding the nails (paronychial), groin, perineum, axillae and inframammary folds where the skin is macerated by perspiration. They appear as scaly, moist lesions with sharply demarcated borders.</p>
<p>Mucosal candidiasis most commonly observed is in the valvovaginal area characterised by a whitish discharge, especially during pregnancy; and oral thrush, in neonates, the elderly and debilitated patients. Oral thrush appears as creamy white patches on the oral mucosa and the tongue.</p>
<p>Systemic candidiasis may involve intestines presenting as a diarrhoea not responding to treatment; pulmonary infection, endocarditis, meningitis, fungaemia and disseminated infection.</p>
<p>&gt; <strong>Note</strong>: Cutaneous and mucosal infections may occur in a normal host whereas systemic infections are most commonly seen in immunocompromised patients.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>Fungal Infections 1107</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="laboratory-diagnosis">Laboratory diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><strong>Specimens</strong> The specimens include swab or scrapings from the cutaneous or mucosal lesions; sputum, CSF, blood, urine, faeces, etc. depending on the body site involved.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="techniques">Techniques<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>(i) Direct Microscopy</p>
<p><strong>Wet mount in saline</strong> <em>Candida</em> appears as oval, budding yeast cells (2–4 μm in diameter) with or without pseudohyphae and hyphae (Fig. 3.2). Appearance of mycelial forms in fresh specimens indicates colonisation and tissue invasion by the yeasts.</p>
<p><strong>Gram stain</strong> The Gram stain of the smear prepared from the specimen shows Gram positive yeast cells, pseudohyphae and hyphae.</p>
<p>(ii) Culture Inoculate the specimens on Sabouraud's agar and incubate at 30°C for 24–48 hours.</p>
<p>Colonies of <em>Candida</em> species appear creamy white, smooth with a yeasty odour.</p>
<p><em>Candida albicans</em> can be identified by the germ-tube test (Fig.3.3) and by the formation of chlamydospores on cornmeal agar (Fig. 3.5). The final identification of the species is made by biochemical tests as shown in Table 3.2.</p>
<p>Table 5.5 Various types of Candidiasis in Man</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Disease process</th><th>Common predisposing factors</th><th>Probable outcome</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Lesions of mucous membranes:</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>(i) Oral thrush</td><td>Infancy, old-age, diabetes, antibiotic therapy, steroid therapy</td><td>Usually self-limiting; may be chronic over many months</td></tr><tr><td>(ii) Vulvovaginal</td><td>Pregnancy, diabetes</td><td>Self-limiting when predisposing condition is cleared</td></tr><tr><td>(iii) Gastrointestinal</td><td>Antibiotic therapy</td><td>Self-limiting after use of antibiotic is discontinued</td></tr><tr><td>(iv) Lesions at corners of the mouth</td><td>Wearing dentures</td><td>May be self-limiting when dentures are properly fitted</td></tr><tr><td>(v) Lesions within folds of tissues such as groin</td><td>Obesity, perspiration</td><td>Clears with aid of mediated powders to keep affected areas dry.</td></tr><tr><td>(vi) Paronychial — lesions around nails</td><td>Maceration</td><td>Usually self-limiting</td></tr><tr><td>Candida granuloma — production of horny, granulomatous lesions</td><td>Immune deficiency</td><td>May respond to antifungal agents, including amphotericin B, atleast temporarily</td></tr><tr><td>Chronic muco-cutaneous candiasis — fungus grows in superficial layers, does not invade past epithelium</td><td>Immune deficiency, unusually cellular or combined</td><td>Recurs after treatment</td></tr><tr><td>Disseminated</td><td>Malignant disease; other serious diseases that compromise host defenses</td><td>Often fatal. May respond to amphotericin B and/or flucytosine</td></tr></tbody></table>
